==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Virtual Reality based serious games and Human Digital Twins have found extensive ap-
plications in the world by providing an more immersive and engaging environment. In
order to integrate Human Digital Twin in serious game according to each person’s dif-
ferent needs. Our research focuses on designing and developing a system, including a
virtual reality game and a web based dashboard to support upper-limb fitness for individu-
als with upper-limb impairment. This thesis covers the research process, from theoretical
background and methodology to system design, implementation, evaluation, and discus-
sion following the principles of agile management structure. It aims to explore how Human
Digital Twins can improve the user experience in serious game-based upper limb fitness,
ultimately enhancing the quality of life for people. Our evaluation was conducted at a
school, with students and therapists, which provided us valuable feedback for the effec-
tiveness and usefulness of our novel Human Digital Twin based upper-limb fitness system
in real world testing. By integrating Human Digital Twins, these games offer a risk-free,
simulated environment for exercises and extensive personalisation, particularly beneficial
for fitness.
keywords: Human Digital Twin, Virtual Reality, Serious Game

Glossary, Acronyms and Abbreviations
API: Application Programming Interface, a set of tools and protocols for building software
applications.
AR: Augmented Reality, technology that overlays digital information on the real world.
BLE: Bluetooth Low Energy, a wireless communication technology designed for short-
range communication with low energy consumption.
CF: Complementary Filter, an algorithm used to combine sensor data for estimating ac-
curate measurements.
GATT: Generic Attribute Profile, a Bluetooth protocol for communication between devices.
GDPR: General Data Protection Regulation, a regulation in EU law on data protection and
privacy.
HDT: Human Digital Twin, a digital counterpart of the physical representation.
IMU: Inertial Measurement Unit, a device that measures and reports a body’s specific
force, angular rate, and sometimes the magnetic field.
MAC: Medium Access Control, a sublayer of the data link layer in the OSI model respon-
sible for controlling how devices on a network gain access to a medium and permission
to transmit data.
MDA: Mechanics, Dynamics, and Aesthetics Framework, a framework used in game de-
sign to analyze games and understand how they work.
MVC: Model-View-Controller, a design pattern used for developing user interfaces by sep-
arating the application into three interconnected components.
NoSQL: Non-relational databases
MVP: Minimum Viable Product, a version of a product with just enough features to be
usable by early customers who can then provide feedback for future development.
QoL: Quality of Life, a term used to evaluate the general well-being of individuals and
societies.
ROM: Range of Motion, the full movement potential of a joint, usually its range of flexion
and extension.
SDK: Software Development Kit, a collection of software development tools in one instal-
lable package.
UI: User Interface, the space where interactions between humans and machines occur.
UX: User experience.
UCD: User Centred Design, a framework of processes in which the needs, wants, and
limitations of end users are given extensive attention at each stage of the design process.
URL: Uniform Resource Locator, the address of a specific webpage or file (such as video,
image, GIF, etc.) on the internet.
UUID: Universally Unique Identifier, a 128-bit number used to uniquely identify informa-
tion in computer systems.
VR: Virtual Reality, a simulated experience that can be similar to or completely different
from the real world.
WCAG: Web Content Accessibility Guidelines.
WebAIM: Web accessibility in mind, a non-profit organisation administering an online
community that focuses on web accessibility.
WebGL: Web Graphics Library, a JavaScript API for rendering interactive 3D graphics
within any compatible web browser.

1
Introduction
In today’s rapidly advancing technological landscape, digital innovations continue to re-
shape our daily lives, enhancing our capabilities and transforming the way we interact with
the world. From smartphones that connect us instantly to the internet of things devices
that make our homes smarter, the integration of digital technology is constantly growing.
One fascinating advancement lies in the field of virtual simulations and digital replicas,
technologies that allow us to create digital twins of physical objects and even humans.
These digital twins offer vast potential, especially in the fields that rely heavily on preci-
sion and personalised interaction, such as aerospace, manufacturing and healthcare. [1,
2, 3]
Specifically in healthcare, Human digital twins (HDT) represents significant leap forward
in personalised healthcare. They integrate data from wearable sensors, medical records,
and other relevant information to create dynamic models of an individual. This model can
then monitor health status, predict disease progression, and tailor treatments or different
processes to each patients unique needs. The potential applications of HDTs are vast,
ranging from chronic disease to personalised rehabilitation [4].
The early applications of digital twin technology was during NASA’s Apollo 13 mission.
An issue occurred with the spacecraft, the team at NASA mirrored the spacecrafts con-
dition, doing this the engineers could test and perfect solutions that safely brought the
astronauts home. This historical example highlights the power of digital twins in criti-
cal, real-time problems [2, 3]. Additionally, the European Commissions, European Virtual
Human Twins Initiative further confirms the importance of this technology. This initiative
aims to improve inclusion and collaboration among stakeholders and enhance research
and development in healthcare through next-generation virtual human twins [5]. However,
the abstract nature of HDTs can be challenging for non-technical users. For HDTs to be
effective, complex data must be presented in an accessible and intuitive way.
Serious games are designed to provide real-world benefits beyond mere entertainment.
These games are widely used in fields such as education, healthcare, and rehabilitation
to help people achieve their goals more effectively. Traditional serious games, integrating
some digital technologies like motion capture and video game, also provide an interactive
nature and a feedback mechanism to help users to be more engaged and allow them to
understand theirs behaviour better. However, they often fall short in providing an immer-
sive and realistic user experience(UX) in actual use due to technological constraints and
customisation challenges. [6]
Virtual Reality (VR) serious games overcome these limitations by creating immersive sce-
narios that engage users more deeply than traditional methods. With the combination of
HDTs, the serious game offers a more risk-free simulated environment for actual prac-
tice and allows for extensive personalisation to meet specific user requirements and skill
levels. Those features particularly beneficial in fitness, enabling more effective and per-
sonalised exercise programs. The data collected during VR-based games also provides
valuable insights for better understanding user needs and tailoring interventions accord-
ingly. [7]
There are various control methods for VR fitness games. Common approaches include
hand controllers and exoskeletons, though other types of hardware and sensors can also
be utilised. Hand controllers are often the default option as they are typically bundled
2
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:12==
with VR headsets, making development straightforward and offering precise control. Ex-
oskeletons, recently used in traditional VR-based serious game studies [8, 9] to support
users’ arms or legs, provide another effective control method that integrates well with es-
tablished fitness exercise. These diverse control options allow for flexibility in tailoring the
exercise experience to individual patient needs and preferences.
Upper limb function is crucial due to its significant impact on the individuals quality of life
(QoL) and overall functionality. The upper limbs is important in performing daily activities,
from basic tasks like eating and dressing to more complex actions that requires fine mo-
tor skills. Difficulties with upper limb function can often lead to considerable challenges,
increasing the need for assistance from a caregiver and thereby reducing a users inde-
pendence. Upper limb function is especially interesting because it addresses these critical
areas, which if helped can provide individuals regain their autonomy, independence and
also improving their QoL. Research has shown that targeted upper limb exercises can not
only enhance motor skills but also help to overall physical fitness and mental well-being.
Conditions such as stroke, traumatic injuries, and neurological disorders often results in
upper limb difficulties, making fitness for strengthening these areas an important factor in
helping people with upper limb difficulties. [10]
Since upper limb functionality is important for daily life, our project focuses on the do-
main of serious game-based upper-limb exercise for people with movement impairments,
referred to as the primary users in this thesis. Our project also includes physical thera-
pists and third-party caregivers, who are normally family members or guardians of primary
users. Although our project builds on previous rehabilitation studies [11, 12, 13, 14], we
aim to extend its application to upper limb fitness, which mainly focuses on improving
fitness but includes elements that potentially benefit upper limb rehabilitation.
Therefore, our integrated system can be used both for rehabilitation and fitness purpose. It
consists of a virtual reality (VR) fitness game and web dashboards as a feedback system.
The VR game utilises an intuitive method of interaction through portable motion sensors,
which offers an improvement over traditional control mechanisms. The web dashboards
comprises three distinct dashboards tailored for the three user groups mentioned above.
These dashboards are interconnected with the VR game, allowing for the configuring
exercises, monitoring progress, and giving feedback.
By combining VR games, motion sensors, a web-based dashboard with HDT, our re-
search aims to explore potential improvements in upper-limb fitness, meeting the needs
of primary users, therapists, and caregivers. This system is designed to enhance the exer-
cising process and collect valuable data for further research. The insights from our study
may contribute to understanding how integrating HDT with serious games can support
fitness outcomes, potentially improving the QoL for users.
The thesis covers our research process, including related research study, design, im-
plementation, evaluation, and discussion. In the Theoretical Background, we examine
existing research and foundational theories relevant to our study. The Methodology sec-
tion describes methods and tools used to manage the project, design, implement, collect
and analyse feedback. The System section describes how we design, develop and in-
tegrate the entire system for the next phase. The Evaluation section describes how we
conducted user testing with students and therapists from a specialised education school
to evaluate, validate and improve the system. The testing user group is a part of our tar-
geted user group. While they had a younger age range and less breadth in physical and
cognitive conditions compared to the targeted user group, they still provided valuable and
relevant feedback for our research. The Discussion chapter discusses the results and
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
3
==PAGE:13==
covers limitations and future works. The Conclusion summarises the key insights of the
project.
Despite the promising conceptual design of combining HDT with VR-based serious games,
significant challenges remain in terms of user accessibility and experience. Developing
these technologies in a way that is widely accessible is still a major hurdle. To address
those challenges, we propose the following research question to explore the potential of
integrating HDTs with VR-based serious games:
How can Human Digital Twins be designed and implemented to enhance
the user experience in serious game-based upper limb fitness?
To further investigate the main research question, we outline the following sub-research
questions:
• How can we design a serious game-based fitness system using HDT to meet user
needs through UX design?
• How can we design and implement a full-stack architecture that is both scalable and
reliable, ensuring the seamless integration and operation of serious games?
• How can gameplay and a VR-sensor-based platform be designed and implemented
to enhance engagement, accessibility, and effectiveness in upper-limb fitness?
• How do different embodiments of the HDT in the developed system affect its effec-
tiveness and the acceptance of the human digital twin concept?
4
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:14==
2
Theoretical Background
This chapter provides a comprehensive overview of the concepts and technologies that
will be used to integrate and design the platform. It aims to provide a foundation for
understanding the key principles that the whole project will be built upon. And afterwards
outline design principles for providing a user centred design and technical frameworks
that can efficiently implement it into a system.
2.1
Human Digital Twin
Traditional rehabilitation for movement and motor difficulties typically requires the exper-
tise of physiotherapists. This process involves one-on-one sessions where patients en-
gage in repetitive, muscle group specific exercises designed to improve motor function in
the targeted area. The therapist evaluates the patients motor impairments and person-
alises the rehabilitation tasks accordingly. An essential part of the rehabilitation process
is the ability to iteratively evaluate the patients condition, modify exercises and adjust the
difficulty of the exercises by providing physical assistance or resistance. This time expen-
sive approach depends heavily on the physiotherapists skill and experience, which can
vary. The need for continuous supervision also limits the feasibility of unsupervised home
rehabilitation. [4]
Because of the evaluation task and adaptability process being a manual task from the
physiotherapists, its not possible to replace them entirely with other technologies. How-
ever, a HDT is a concept that has emerged as a powerful tool in enhancing rehabilitation
processes. Specifically for healthcare, an HDT is a database or real-time collection of a
patients actual condition, which can be represented in various ways. This tool can allow
therapists to better plan tasks, and to a degree, automate patient evaluations [4]. It repre-
sents a new digital approach in monitoring physical activity that can be used together with
other advanced technologies. HDT utilises data from a medium that can have a range
of sensors, including accelerometers, gyroscopes, heart rate monitors, cameras, etc. to
collect comprehensive data about the patients movement and physiological responses.
Additionally, the use of machine learning and AI algorithms can further process the col-
lected data, enabling analysis and feedback directly to the therapist. This allows for more
in depth statistics and performance metrics that can be used to adjust the personalised re-
habilitation plan. It also provides data that an application can base its difficulty on through
the patients real-time performance. By reducing to some extent the tasks that a phys-
iotherapists needs to perform manually, HDTs can free up resources, allowing focus on
more detailed and personalised plans or the time to manage more patients simultane-
ously. Additionally, HDTs can facilitate unsupervised home exercise by providing remote
monitoring and feedback, making rehabilitation more accessible and flexible. [15, 16]
Specifically in coaching, which a physiotherapist also do, measuring an athletes perfor-
mance is already widely practised, especially in professional contexts where any form of
improvement is valuable. A case study by Barricelli et al. [17] shows how HDT is used
in a practical setting. This study demonstrates how the technology was used to monitor
and manage the fitness activities of athletes, specifically a non-professional soccer team.
The SmartFit framework used in this study uses IoT sensors embedded in wearables,
to collect data on various fitness-related parameters, such as activity levels, heart rate,
sleep patterns, and food intake. The collected data was then analysed using advanced
machine learning algorithms, which allowed the HDTs to predict the athletes performance
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
5
==PAGE:15==
and provide personal feedback. The feedback then suggested optimisation for different
training routines and identifying areas for improvements. Their findings was that the im-
plementation of the SmartFit framework showed significant improvements in the athletes
performance statistics. Other smart devices such as smart soles has also been developed
to analyse how a soccer player moves around on the field, allowing for the identification
of movements that can be optimised or areas the athlete needs to be aware of [15]. Simi-
larly, skiing has smart soles that reduces the need for personal coaching. This is done by
having the soles connected to an application, and after each run the app will provide the
user with an statistical overview of the users performance, while also offering suggestions
for improvements [18]. These technologies reduces the need for a personal coach for mi-
nor technique refinements, requiring their assistance only for significant improvements.
Another example is a wearable vest that have electrodes and sensors which can detect
the wearers physiological signals in real-time [15]. These technologies shows that an as-
pect of a digital twin is already widely used to some extent, and that the more information
a system can collect from sensors, can result in a more accurate and functional a HDT.
Despite the potential of HDTs, their abstract nature is a significant challenge for user ac-
ceptance and understanding. The concept of a digital replica that continuously updates
and provides feedback can be difficult for users to comprehend. This abstraction can lead
to resistance in adoption and lack of trust in the digital counterpart. Therefore, it is crucial
to design the HDTs in a way that makes them tangible and intuitive for users. According
to ”I Feel You” research has shown that humans often struggle with abstract represen-
tation of data and prefer more concrete, visual and interactive methods of understanding
complex information. For instance, in healthcare, patients may find it challenging to com-
prehend quantitative data without contextual or qualitative representations. Designing
HDTs with user-friendly interfaces that utilises information visualisation can help reduce
the gap making the abstract concept more tangible. Typical design principles would in
this context be used to design tangible models. [16]
2.2
Embodiment of Information Systems
As mentioned, the concept of a HDT can be very abstract and it is therefore crucial to de-
sign the HDT in a way that makes data more tangible and intuitive for users. Embodiment
in the context of this thesis will be used as the expression to give a tangible or percep-
tible form to something abstract. Described by Zhao and Vande Moere in ”Embodying
Information Systems: A Model of the Physical Visualization of Information” [19] traditional
screen-based information visualisation is unable to capture and convey the rich sensory
experiences due to the limitations of a simple screen. They argue that human interactions
in the real world are inherently multi-modal, utilising auditory, visual and tactile senses.
They suggests that data sculptures can solve the absence of this aspect by introducing
physical artefacts that can be touched, explored and interacted with, similar to everyday
objects. They propose that data sculptures serve as data-based physical artefacts that
has functional qualities of both aspects. They present a model (Appendix G) that mea-
sures the metaphorical distance between data and its physical representation. The closer
the metaphorical distance the more intuitive and engaging the representation becomes
for a user.
2.2.1
The Concept of Embodiment
As computers are effective at doing repetitive work, processing huge amount of data in-
stantaneously and finding patterns in numbers itself, we humans have a difficult time in
perceiving this type of information. Therefore finding a medium between what computers
and humans excel at is crucial [20, 16]. The concept of embodying information systems
6
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:16==
can be understood through phenomenology, as discussed by John Mingers in his paper
”Embodying Information Systems: Contribution of Phenomenology” [20]. Mingers de-
scribes that traditional information systems have been built around Cartesian dualism,
where the physical body and the cognitive mind is separated and seen as two individual
parts. This approach, has been a fundamental part of why current information systems
has been limited to a simple screen. He mentions that the embodiment of information sys-
tems involves several key elements. Systems should facilitate natural interactions through
physical actions. This means similar to Zhao and Vande Moere, interfaces should utilise
touch, movement, and other body interactions to make data more tangible and intuitive.
Systems should be aware of the users context, including their physical environment. This
can enhance the relevance and usability of the information provided. Incorporating multi-
ple sensory inputs such as visual, auditory and haptic, can help make abstract data more
concrete and tangible. [20, 19, 16]
Nancy Krieger’s work also supports and extends these ideas by emphasising that embod-
iment is an active and engaged process. According to her, embodiment in epidemiology
refers to how we literally incorporate the physical world in which we live in, including the
societal conditions. This concept highlights that the human body tells stories about our
lives and environments, making it essential for information systems to consider the users
physical context to truly be effective. Her theory suggests that embodiment is a multi-level
phenomenon integrating biological, psychological and social dimensions, and thereby fur-
ther proves the need for systems to capture and convey all these aspects. [21]
Polani, et al. further elaborates on the connection between information processing, em-
bodiment, and interaction with environment. They argue that the traditional perspective on
Artificial Intelligence (AI) is being changed. Previously, AI was often viewed as a cognitive
process without an understanding of its physical and environmental contexts. However,
for organisms the physical perception and environmental information are integral to the
process of understanding. They propose that embodied intelligence recognises cognition
and action as emerging from the interaction between brain, body and the environment.
This perspective highlights that intelligent behaviour cannot be fully understood or repli-
cated without considering embodiment of agents. [22]
What the papers highlight is the importance of integrating physical and environmental
contexts into information processing systems to enhance its effectiveness and tangibility.
And thereby the concept of embodiment transforms an abstract object into a tangible one.
2.2.2
Common Embodiment Techniques
Through a model (Appendix G) Zhao and Vande Moere highlight several key elements
from the data sculpture model. By allowing users to physically interact with data sculptures
it utilises the natural human ability in understanding and manipulating physical objects.
This interaction can make abstract data more tangible and engaging. Therefore, multi-
sensory engagement through multiple senses beyond just the visual, can enhance the
overall user experience and make the interaction more memorable. By also designing
the data sculptures such as texture, shape and material can provide affordances that can
guide users in how to interact with them. These aspects will help the users to intuitively
understand the data through physical interaction. [19]
An abstract subject like physics can also be difficult to understand and comprehend. A
research demonstrates that higher levels of embodiment in educational activities, such
as using motion capture and gesture-based interactions, lead to greater learning and
higher engagement compared to traditional text-based methods. Educational technolo-
gies which incorporates high levels of embodiment where students actively use gestures
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
7
==PAGE:17==
to interact with content, can transform the learning experience. Like using a Microsoft
Kinect sensor to track body movements which allows students to manipulate virtual ob-
jects that mirrors real-world actions, and thus making an abstract scientific concept more
concrete and tangible. Findings suggests that designing tools with these principles in
mind can significantly improve the efficiency of learning environments. [23]
Technologies such as Virtual Reality (VR) and Augmented Reality (AR) can provide im-
mersive experiences that embody information in a tangible way. They provide feedback
that users can easily understand and act upon, integrating seamlessly into typical interac-
tions. Haptic feedback systems uses touch sensations to convey information, and earlier
mentioned technologies such as the wearable vest can provide physical ques to guide
users, and thus making abstract instruction more tangible. Information should therefore
be designed with a focus on human experiences, recognising that the user is not just
a passive recipients of information but actually active users that interact with the data
through their physical bodies. User interfaces should be intuitive and aligned with human
cognitive and physical capabilities. [20]
2.3
Serious Game for Health
Serious Game is a type of game designed for specific purposes beyond entertainment,
such as developing certain skills. These games are structured with several rules that
players must engage with to improve their skills. They go beyond mere entertainment, fo-
cusing on education, training, and health improvement, and emphasize core components
like objectives and technology. Typically, serious games contain the following compo-
nents[24]:
• Implicit Objective. The ultimate purpose of the serious game, such as improving
players’ abilities or gaining knowledge.
• Explicit Objective. The direct goal in the game, such as beating an opponent or
racing to the finish line.
• Rule or Gameplay. The guidelines that dictate how the game is played and what
actions players can take.
• Challenge. The difficulty level in the game designed to motivate players.
• Interaction. The action players control the game, such as typing, button pressing,
full-body control, voice control, etc.
Since serious games can aid in skill development in an engaging and motivating man-
ner, they are increasingly utilized in the healthcare area. For example, these games can
transform standard fitness exercises into interactive games, enabling users to engage in
gameplay while performing their exercises. Wattanasoontorn et al. [24] conducted an
analysis of 108 serious games, categorizing them based on their purpose, functional-
ity, stage of disease, and the wellness of the players. This study provided an extensive
overview of the use of serious games in healthcare:
• Purpose. Games were categorized as focused on entertainment, health, or the
acquisition of health and medical skills.
• Functionality. Considerations included cognitive skills, motor skills, interaction tech-
nology, game interface, number of players, and difficulty adaptability.
• Stage of disease. Games were classified according to the susceptibility stage, pre-
symptomatic stage, and clinical disease stage.
8
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:18==
• Player’s wellness. The target audience was identified as either patients or non-
patients.
Astrojumper, a virtual reality (VR) game, incorporates full-body movements such as dodg-
ing and jumping, effectively increasing heart rates and physical activity. A study involv-
ing 30 participants demonstrated high levels of motivation and enjoyment, indicating the
game’s success in engaging users. In addition to encouraging users, serious games can
also integrate advanced technologies to enhance their effectiveness. [25]
RehaBot, an adaptive rehabilitation game framework, provides personalised and adap-
tive telerehabilitation experiences using serious games. The system employs data mining
techniques to forecast improvement, evaluates multiple performance indicators, and ad-
justs exercises based on user data. It accurately tracks posture and offers intelligent alerts
for gameplay instructions, taking the solution to the next level. [26]
The discussion above shows that serious games, designed with specific objectives be-
yond entertainment, is a promising approach to improving health outcomes through en-
gaging and interactive methods.
2.4
Upper-Limb Fitness
Even though typical workout often involves the entire body, focusing specifically on upper
limb is crucial for several reasons. For individuals with significant in motor functions of
the upper limbs, enhancing these functions can substantially improve a their quality of
life (QoL). Restoring essential abilities such as reaching, grasping, and fine motor skills
directly impacts a patients ability to perform daily activities independently. Activities like
dressing, eating, and personal hygiene require coordinated arm and hand movements.
Improving upper limb function through training can reduce dependency on caregivers or
parents, and boosting self-esteem. Research indicates that disorders affecting the upper
limbs significantly impact a patients physical activity, mental state and overall QoL. These
motor impairments become more prevalent with age and are a leading cause of disability.
The goals for patients with these disorders now extend beyond reducing their symptoms,
to also including increasing their functional abilities and enhancing QoL. By improving
users upper limb function, it has a greater potential to also increase their overall QoL. [10]
Upper limb exercise is beneficial even if it does not involve the entire body. Studies have
shown that upper limb fitness can lead to significant physical exertion and energy expen-
diture, contributing to the users overall physical fitness. The study by Graves et al. [27]
showed that active video gaming, such as playing on the Nintendo Wii, resulted in sub-
stantial increases in energy usage and heart rate compared to other stationary activities.
This suggests that upper limb exercises can still provide considerable physical exertion,
making them a valuable component of rehabilitation programs. The study specifically
found that upper limb exercise during video gaming increased movement, highlighting the
motivational aspect and enhances physical exertion. This indicates that such exercises
can be physically demanding and effective in improving overall fitness, further supporting
its effectiveness. [27, 28]
There are a number of conditions and incidents that can result in upper-limb movement
impairment, including stroke, traumatic injuries, neurological disorders, musculoskeletal
conditions, autism, and congenital dysplasia [29, 30]. Furthermore, Some of these dis-
eases are often accompanied by lower extremity disabilities and cognitive problems. For
example, upper limb disability is a common problem for people with neurological disabili-
ties [31]. And for people with stroke or a spinal cord injury, disorders of the upper extremity
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
9
==PAGE:19==
are often accompanied by lower extremity disabilities [8]. Autism spectrum is also char-
acterised by motor delays and impairments that often precede atypical development [32].
Congenital dysplasia may also cause cognitive difficulties in some children [33].
A key concept in upper limb rehabilitation and fitness is the Range of Motion (ROM). The
ROM of the upper limb, encompassing the wrist, elbow, and shoulder, is crucial for per-
forming everyday tasks. Essential movements include shoulder flexion, shoulder exten-
sion, shoulder abduction, shoulder adduction, elbow flexion, and elbow extension, among
others [34]. Limitations in ROM can significantly impact one’s ability to carry out daily ac-
tivities effectively. Engaging in fitness exercises can help enhancing ROM and improving
overall functionality.
Focusing on upper limb fitness can be sufficient to exert patients physically while providing
the added benefit of targeted improvement in essential motor functions. This approach
allows for concentrated efforts on enhancing upper limb capabilities, which are crucial for
daily activities and overall QoL.
2.5
Design Principles
2.5.1
User-Centred Design
The User-Centred Design (UCD) is a framework for iteratively researching, designing, and
evaluating services and systems by involving end users and other stakeholders through-
out a project life cycle [35]. UCD is important for ensuring that the solutions developed
are user-friendly and meet the actual needs of the users.
2.5.1.1
User-Centred Design in Healthcare
UCD in healthcare emphasises involving users. In healthcare innovation, the engagement
of diverse stakeholders is essential to the development of a shared agenda for responsible
innovation and for the co-creation of social value[36]. The forms of involvement vary
from collecting extensive data to asking individuals to assess early products. The level
of involvement can also be adjusted between informing (as in interviews), advising (as in
reviewing concepts), and doing (as in having stakeholders co-perform research or design
work) [37].
Design approaches to healthcare plays a crucial role for the research process of related
projects. It promises to aid the development of innovative, effective, and person-centred
solutions to health challenges, supporting the realisation of a future for health care that is
preventative, personalised, and participatory in nature [38].
For our project, UCD ensures that the fitness exercises we design for people with upper-
limb movement impairments are accessible, user-friendly. Helping address their specific
healthcare needs by involving them in the design and evaluation process, to enhance the
usability of the system.
2.5.2
Universal Design
Universal design is an approach that aims to create products and environments that are
usable by people with a wide range of abilities, disabilities, and characteristics. It goes
beyond accommodating specific user groups and focuses on designing for the broadest
possible range of users. In the context of digital product UX/UI design, universal design
ensures that interfaces and interactions are accessible, intuitive, and inclusive for every-
one. It aims to create products and environments that are usable by people with a wide
range of abilities, disabilities, and characteristics. [39]
To ensure our system meets the needs of users with upper-limb movement impairments
10
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:20==
and potential cognitive limitations, we follow universal design principles, to create an ac-
cessible and user-friendly system for varying physical and cognitive abilities.
In Web Content Accessibility Guidelines (WCAG) 2.2, the principles were presented as
the foundation necessary for anyone to access and use web content. Here we mainly
introduce three of them:
• Perceivable: Information and user interface components must be presentable to
users in ways they can perceive, which means that users must be able to perceive
the information presented; it cannot be invisible to their senses. [40]
• Operable: User interface components and navigation must be operable, so that
Users are able to operate the interface. [40]
• Understandable: Information and the operation of user interface must be under-
standable, which means that Users are able to understand both the information and
the operation of the user interface. [40]
These principles outline the basic standards to ensure the system is accessible to most
people. Additionally, they help us understand the minimum requirements for our target
users, ensuring they have the basic abilities necessary to use the system effectively. For
example, the system is designed for users who can perceive visual information and have
sufficient cognitive ability to understand and navigate the interface.
2.5.2.1
Cognitive Accessibility
Web accessibility in mind (WebAIM)’s guidelines on cognitive disabilities proposed that
individuals with cognitive or learning disabilities may use a wide variety of technologies to
adapt or simplify content to their needs. Text-to-speech, customised fonts or page colours,
text simplification, and other techniques might be used. Even though such technologies
are available, designer and developers must also consider the design of their content
to minimise cognitive difficulty. We mainly introduce the three principles in WebAIM’s
guidelines to enhance the user experiences of our systems for cover potential users with
cognitive disabilities [41]:
• Accommodate Problem-Solving Deficits: This involves avoiding complex problem-
solving requirements, simplifying navigation, and providing clear, concise instruc-
tions to help users avoid frustration. [41]
• Accommodate Attention Deficits: Techniques include minimising distractions, using
simple and clean designs, and ensuring that critical information stands out without
unnecessary clutter. [41]
• Accommodate Visual Comprehension Deficits: Approaches include providing alter-
native text for images, ensuring that visual elements are easily recognisable, and
using clear and distinguishable icons. [41]
These principles of cognitive accessibility are essential because they provide a framework
for users with cognitive impairments, who are a part of our target groups. By keeping
that in mind throughout our design process, we ensure the System is inclusive and user-
friendly for all users.
2.6
Technical Frameworks and Tools
In order to create something that can incorporate the previous mentioned theory, a system
needs to be made which can facilitate this. The objective of this chapter is to provide
and highlight important frameworks and technologies that can be utilised to implement a
system that supports the theory as mentioned in the sections before.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
11
==PAGE:21==
2.6.1
Full Stack Development
Full stack development involves creating both the front-end and back-end components of
an application. Every application consists of a front-end (user-facing) component and a
back-end (database and logic) component. The front-end includes the UI and manages
user interactions with the application. The back-end contains all the code required for the
application to function, including data integration, communication with other applications,
and data processing. While front-end and back-end development require distinct skills,
tools, and software knowledge, full stack development merges both disciplines. This in-
tegration allows development teams to build applications from start to finish in a cohesive
manner. A full stack application contains both front-end and back-end elements within a
single code base. [42, 43]
The technologies used in full stack development vary depending on the focus area. Front-
end technology focuses on the client-facing side of development, coding the graphical UI
and interactive features. Common front-end languages include Hypertext Markup Lan-
guage (HTML), Cascading Style Sheets (CSS), and JavaScript (JS). HTML structures
web pages, CSS styles the content, and JS adds interactive features. Back-end technol-
ogy manages the exchange of information between the front-end and the server. Com-
mon back-end languages include Hypertext Preprocessor, Ruby, Java, and Python, etc.
Back-end technology can be grouped into three sub-layers: the Application Programming
Interface (API) layer, the storage layer, and the business logic layer. The API layer acts
as a bridge between the front-end and back-end, handling user interactions. The storage
layer manages data storage and communication with databases. The business logic layer
is the core of the back-end, processing logic and determining API request responses. [42,
43]
2.6.2
Front-end
2.6.2.1
React
React is a popular JS library developed and maintained by Facebook. It is primarily used
for building UIs, especially for single-page applications where creating a dynamic and
responsive user experience is essential. Unlike other frameworks that handle multiple
aspects of an application, React focuses specifically on the UI, providing developers with
flexibility to use it alongside other tools and libraries. React simplifies the development of
complex UI by breaking them down into smaller, reusable components. Each component
is a self-contained unit that manages its own content and behaviour. These components
can then be combined to form larger applications, making the development process more
manageable and organised. It uses a unique approach to updating the UI efficiently.
Instead of directly manipulating the web pages structure (Document Object Model(DOM)),
it works with a virtual representation of the DOM (Figure 2.1). When changes occur, React
updates the virtual DOM first, then figures out the most efficient way to update the actual
DOM. This results in faster and smoother updates, enhancing the performance of the
application. Another feature of React is JavaScript XML which allows developers to write
HTML-like code within JS. This makes the code more readable and easier to understand.
By integrating HTML with JS it allows the developers to write components that closely
mirrors the final rendered output [43]. It is a powerful tool for building UI. It focuses on
re-usability, performance and flexibility.
Besides React, Angular and Vue are also frameworks and libraries that has similar func-
tionalities. Angular, which is developed by Google, is essentially a all-in-one approach
which provides robust structure for developing complex application. The programming
language for Angular is TypeScript, it is an extension of JS, making it more suited for
large-scale applications [45]. However, due to its extensive feature set, Angular has a
12
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:22==
Figure 2.1: The tree structure of a DOM. React utilises a virtual DOM to identify and
update only the nodes that need to be affected when changes occur [44].
steeper learning curve compared to React and Vue. Alternatively, Vue is similar to React,
known for its simplicity and ease of integration. It uses a component-based architecture
and JS, making it easy to learn and quick to implement. Vue also uses virtual DOM to
efficiently update web pages, resulting in similar performance alike React. Its flexibility
makes it a great choice for smaller projects or applications that requires fast implementa-
tion. [46]
For an application intended for this project, either React or Vue would be suitable for the
front-end. Due to developer familiarity, React will be the chosen framework.
2.6.2.2
Next.js
Next.js is a robust React framework, it is designed to enhance the capabilities of React
by providing an array of features aimed at simplifying and optimising the development of
modern web applications. Unlike React, which focuses solely on building UI, Next.js offers
a full-fledged framework that supports server-side rendering (SSR), static site generation
(SSG) and client-side rendering, making it an ideal choice for creating both dynamic and
static websites. One of the main features of Next.js is SSR, which allows React com-
ponents to be rendered on the server before being sent to the client. This makes web
applications load faster because the content is fully rendered when it reaches the client.
It also supports SSG, which creates static HTML pages at build time. This approach is
beneficial for content-driven websites, as it combines the speed of static sites with the dy-
namic capabilities of React. The framework simplifies routing through a file-based system,
where pages are directly mapped to the file structure within the ”pages” directory. This
makes it easy to manage routes and navigate between different parts of the application.
[47]
Next.js extends the capabilities of React by offering SSR, SSG and simplifies develop-
ment. And is the obvious choice when React is chosen as one of the frameworks. If Vue
was chosen instead, then the equivalent of Next.js for Vue would be Nuxt.js.

that has been reached on how to design the interactions of these universal components.
Even though the developers could not find a suitable component for their projects, they
could still feel free to customised those components as material ui provides a great deal of
freedom in redesigning these components. In addition to supporting those easy-for-use

2.6.3
Application Programming Interfaces
An Application Programming Interface (API), is a set of rules and protocols that allows
different software applications to communicate with each other. APIs act as the connec-
tion between the front-end and back-end, enabling the front-end to retrieve, send, and
manipulate data stored on the server.
2.6.3.1
Graphics Renderer
Displaying 3D content on web pages can be extensive when using Web Graphics Library
(WebGL), as it requires extensive coding just to manage points, lines and triangles. This
process of using WebGL is simplified by Three.js, a framework built on top of WebGL
that makes it easier to create 3D scenes. Three.js allows developers to manage complex
graphics with basic JS knowledge. The setup of using Three.js is similar to other renderer,
it involves a renderer and a camera view that decides which part of a scene is rendered
and displayed on the web. Within this scene, objects such as lights, shadows, materials,
textures, 3D models, and animations are framed to the developers intention. [50]
2.6.3.2
Restful API
Rest, the Representational State Transfer, is a software architecture style created by Roy
Fielding in 2000 to guide the architectural design of the network, it is a standard and
versatile way for the communication between microservices with the clients, mainly used
among web applications to control services and manage the digital business under the
guidance of HTTP(Hypertext Transfer Protocol). All the api designs that follows the REST
design principles would be called RESTful api, the core of the Restful api is to uniform in-
terface and make those interfaces more readable and accessible. And all the requests
are independent from each other, which means that all the requests would not be inter-
rupted by others, making programs extension easily. The workflow would be shown in
the (Figure 2.2). [51]

for using. And when constructing the springboot application, it would support the depen-

and management. In this way, no manual configuration required, the developer could

2.6.4.2
Redis
Redis is a memory database integrating cache, session storage and so on, it stores data
by generating key-value pairs. It provides various data type that is easy to be applied for
different scenarios, also convenient for developer to extend[53]. Redis is a single thread
model with well performance, it processes the concurrent requests very fast by listening
to every client’s I/O states simultaneously, which would prevent I/O blocking and avoid
the competition in multi-threading model, at the same time, it stores the data in memory
that enhances the utility of CPU. Then such data structure in storage and single thread
model ensure the high performance and low latency, also simplifies the complexity of
concurrency control when using Redis2.3. [54]
2.6.4.3
MongoDB Atlas and MongoDB Realm
MongoDB is a NoSQL database and the MongoDB Atlas is to host the MongoDB in cloud
which is easier to deploy and operate. By using NoSQL, the developer does not need to
create relationships between different collection. MongoDB provides a more versatile way
to handle the database, since there is no strict relation between collections, it is very flex-
ible for developer to process their data, also improved software project’s maintainability.
[55]
Compare to SQL(Relational Data Stream Management System), the NoSQL database
like MongoDB is more suitable for our system. It is easy to process horizontal scaling to
support the read and write for amount of data, and also enables multiple different data
types stored in the database, which effectively saves the development time. Besides, the
format of the data that store in MongoDB document is JSON, it makes the store struc-
ture very flexible and easy to expand, then provides the index for data query, which also
guarantees data query speed. [56]
Apart from having considered the performance of the MongoDB, it also provides an em-
bedded database for mobile platform - MongoDB Realm. Since the unity game also needs
to read and write the data in the database to keep the data consistency, the MongoDB
would be the best choice for our system.

applications, where allows the developer to create the images for their projects. It also pro-

picture uploads, generating avatars based on the uploaded images. As it supports cross-
platform use it is ideal for maintaining the avatar appearance consistent across multiple
platforms. The platform itself features an intuitive web-based interface offering real-time
previews of the avatars. It can easily be integrated into projects using APIs and Software
Development Kits (SDK), supporting graphics platform like Unity, Unreal and WebGL.
Ready Player Me simplifies avatar management and ensures updates are reflected across
all platforms that uses it [58]. Avaturn is another similar platform that can create and
manage realistic 3D avatars by uploading pictures of the user. However, it does not allow
users to create an avatar that is not based on the user uploaded pictures of themselves,
making it less flexible compared to Ready Player Me [59]. Despite this, Avaturn excels in
generating highly realistic avatars, providing superior realism.
Due to the flexibility of Ready Player Me, specifically its ability to create avatars without
uploading personal images, it the chosen technology. As it allows the user to have an
active choice if they would prefer to look as they do now, or maybe wants to be something
they are not.
Inworld.ai [60] is a sophisticated tool that leverages AI technology to enhance avatars
in gaming environments. It creates AI NPCs (Non-player Characters) that respond dy-
namically to players’ actions in real-time, featuring lifelike facial and body animations and
the ability to speak AI-generated or pre-defined content. Inworld.ai is fully compatible with
popular game engines like Unity, simplifying its integration into various game development
workflows.
Inworld.ai also integrates with other advanced tools, such as Ready Player Me, which
provides customizable 3D avatar models. Combining Ready Player Me with Inworld.ai al-
lows developers to create richly detailed, user-defined 3D avatars that interact intelligently
16
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:26==
within the game environment. This synergy ensures that avatars are visually appealing
and capable of contributing to an immersive and dynamic gameplay experience.
2.6.6
Virtual Reality Game
2.6.6.1
Virtual Reality
VR is a transformative technology that enables users to immerse themselves in a three-
dimensional (3D) virtual environment, simulating real-world experiences. Michael Heim
summarised the characteristic of Virtual Reality, known as ”Three I’s”, immersion, in-
teractivity, and information intensity [61]. This technology typically comprises a set of
hardware components, including head-mounted displays (HMDs), headphones, motion-
sensing gloves or hand controllers to generate and render the virtual worlds. These com-
ponents work together to create a cohesive and interactive experience, allowing users to
engage with the virtual environment in a highly realistic and immersive manner. [62]
To create a realistic and immersive embodiment of HDT, VR surpasses other gaming
platforms. By placing a human avatar in the VR game, users can interact in a way that
enhances their emotional and psychological attachment to the game. Additionally, VR
creates a simulated immersive environment that closely resembles real-world objects and
settings, allowing users to move and interact with virtual objects similarly to how they
would in the real world. This technology can produce a sensory illusion of real-world
simulation within a highly controlled environment, providing users with a deeply engaging
and realistic experience. Through its advanced capabilities, VR not only offers superior
immersion and interaction but also facilitates the creation of sophisticated digital twins
that can be used for various applications beyond gaming, such as training, education,
and health interventions [63]. Users participate in this virtual world rather than merely
using it, making the experience more intuitive [64].
However, there are also side effects that need to be addressed. VR can result in cyber-
sickness [65] in certain circumstances, which is the onset of nausea during virtual expe-
riences, often accompanied by symptoms such as dizziness and headaches. Another
similar term is Visually Induced Motion Sickness (VIMS), which refers to nausea caused
by visual stimuli. The most common theory regarding the cause of these conditions is
sensory mismatch, where stimuli are perceived differently by various senses, leading to
a discordant experience that triggers these symptoms. Therefore, while VR offers sig-
nificant advantages in the context of serious games for health, it is essential to develop
strategies and technologies to minimise these side effects to ensure a safe and effective
user experience.
2.6.6.2
Virtual Reality Products
Choosing a specific VR product for fitness requires careful consideration, specially the end
user, product accessibility and compatibility with other technical frameworks. An important
aspect to consider is the distinction between inside-out and outside-in tracking methods
in VR. This relates to how the VR system monitors and interpret the users movement and
position within the virtual environment. The tracking ensures that the virtual experience
responds accurately to the users actions, which helps to provide an immersive and inter-
active experience. Therefore, the effectiveness of tracking is therefore crucial to maintain
a sense of realism and seamless interaction in the virtual environment.
Inside-out tracking uses cameras and sensors that are built into the VR headset itself.
Theses sensors detect the user’s movement and the surrounding environment without
requiring external hardware. This approach simplifies the setup process, making it a easy
plug and play solution. Additionally, inside-out tracking also offers much better portability,
the users are only required to pack the headset itself and the controllers, making it much
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
17
==PAGE:27==
more flexible and convenient. [66, 67] On the other hand, outside-in tracking relies on
external hardware, such as base stations, which is placed around the users play area.
These base stations track the position and movement of the VR headset and controllers
with higher precision. The benefit of this is primarily in the precision and consistency,
as tracking from multiple base stations provides more stable and accurate detection of
user movement from different angles. The high level of accuracy is especially beneficial
for applications requiring detailed or fine movement tracking, making outside-in tracking
better for professional settings, where accuracy is important. [66, 68, 69]
Both tracking methods offer unique advantages. Inside-out is favoured due to its sim-
plicity and portability, great for home. In contrast, outside-in tracking is better regarding
precision and stability. However, many of the current headsets now ship with with inside-
out tracking, and offer the option to purchase base-stations to improve precision. This
flexibility means that the choice of headset should not be based solely on the tracking
method, but rather on compatibility with other technical frameworks that will be used.
2.6.6.3
Unity Game Engine
To develop a VR game, a game engine is essential. A game engine is a software frame-
work designed for game development, providing a suite of tools and libraries for various
components such as rendering, physics, sound, animation, threading, networking, and
user interfaces. It includes an editor to compose game objects and manages the com-
pilation and deployment to multiple platforms, including PC, web, Android, iOS, etc. By
using a game engine, developers can focus on creating game interaction logic without the
burden of implementing fundamental effects and features from scratch.
In this project, we use Unity engine, a dominant choice for developing VR games. It sup-
ports various VR platforms and is built internally with C/C++, ensuring high performance.
On top of this, Unity utilises an open-source .NET framework, allowing developers to write
code in high-level, object-oriented languages like C# or JavaScript, and leverage .NET
libraries. This combination of performance and flexibility makes Unity a powerful tool for
VR game development. [70]
2.6.7
Motion Sensor
Typically, players use a pair of motion-sensing gloves or hand controllers along with a
headset to play VR games. However, holding controllers can make it challenging for
users to perform exercises simultaneously. To enable users to play VR games while
exercising, or more technically, to use exercise movements to control and play the VR
game, alternative motion-sensing hardware is necessary.
Integrating motion sensors, such as Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU) [71], into VR sys-
tems can address this issue. This sensor is characterised by its small size, lightweight
design, wireless communication, and low power consumption, making it highly suitable
for mounting on the human body or various pieces of equipment using bands.
IMU is extensively utilised across a wide range of applications, from smartphones to air-
craft, due to their ability to accurately measure velocity, rotation, orientation, and gravita-
tional force. It comprises an accelerometer and a gyroscope. The accelerometer compo-
nent measures inertial acceleration, including the effects of gravity, while the gyroscope
measures angular rotation (angle per second). Both the accelerometer and gyroscope of-
fer three degrees of freedom, which correspond to the three-dimensional axes (x, y, and z)
in the real world. This capability allows for precise tracking of movement and orientation.
Communication between the motion sensors and the game is facilitated by Bluetooth
Low Energy (BLE), a feature in Bluetooth 4.0 standard with lower power consumption
18
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.5: IMU Measurement of linear acceleration and angular motion [72]
compared to classical Bluetooth (Bluetooth 3.0). Nowadays, all PCs and smartphones
natively support BLE. This low power consumption makes BLE ideal for small devices
with a small battery that can last for years. BLE supports two communication methods:
broadcasting and connections. Broadcasting sends data to any devices within listening
range quickly, while connections send data between two devices in a private connection.
Applications of BLE include heart rate sensors, kinematic tracking, and IMU sensors,
among others.[73, 74]
By integrating these sensors onto the human body or equipment, it becomes possible to
achieve a more intuitive and natural method of interaction within VR games. This setup
facilitates hands-free operation, thereby enhancing user immersion and engagement. The
ability to track subtle movements and orientations accurately provides a more seamless
and realistic experience in virtual environments. This integration not only enhances the
gaming experience by making it more immersive and natural but also promotes physical
activity by seamlessly incorporating exercise into the gameplay. Utilizing IMU for motion-
sensing thus represents a significant advancement in VR technology, enabling a more
holistic and engaging user experience.
2.6.8
Bluetooth Low Energy Protocol
The motion sensor uses Bluetooth Low Energy(BLE) communication protocol. To under-
stand BLE communication, we first introdcue the complete BLE protocol stack. According
to Tosi[73] and Gomez[74], BLE protocol consists of Controller layer and Host layer. Host
Controller Interface (HCI) serves as the middle end between Controller layer and Host
layer.
The Controller layer includes Physical layer and Link layer.
• Physical layer operates in 2.4 GHz Industrial Scientific Medical (ISM) band with
a variation of 40 Radio Frequency(RF) channels spaced 2 MHz. The channels is
divided to 2 types, advertising channels for searching device and broadcasting, data
channels for private communication between connected devices.
• Link layer defines the device role as either master or slave. The slave device, also
known as the advertiser, uses advertising channels to broadcast its connectability.
The master device, also referred to as the initiator, listens for these advertisements
and can send a connection request to the slave device, establishing a point-to-point
connection via data channels. This connection forms a star topology network, known
as a piconet, where a master can connect to multiple slaves simultaneously, while
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
19
==PAGE:29==
Figure 2.6: BLE Protocol Stack
each slave can connect to only one master. In our system, the motion sensors act
as slaves, and the VR headset functions as the master.
Host layer includes Logical Link Control and Adaptation Protocol (L2CAP), Security Man-
ager Protocol (SMP), Attribute Protocol (ATT), Generic Attribute Profile (GATT), Generic
Access Profile (GAP).
• L2CAP can encapsulate data from Link layer into BLE standard packets and multi-
plex to ATT and SMP, and vice versa.
• SMP contains security algorithms for master and slave respectively to encrypt and
decrypt data.
• ATT defines the device role into client or server in terms of client-server architecture,
independent of slave or master role defined in Link layer. It also organizes the
data into attributes by assigning a handle, a Universally Unique Identifier (UUID),
permissions with the value.
• GATT also defines role into client and server corresponding to ATT. It clearly spec-
ifies a high-level data hierarchy comprising components called services, with each
service grouping data into components called characteristics. A characteristic con-
tains properties (such as broadcast, readable, writable, or notifiable), descriptors,
and the data value itself. Each service and characteristic is distinguished by UUID.
Manufacturers can define these services themselves, allowing for convenient adap-
tation to different use cases. For the Syncsense motion sensor we used, it includes
a motion service and a battery service (Figure 2.7). The motion service provides
motion data for game input. The motion data, including acceleration and gyroscope
information, is notifiable. This means that when a device subscribes to the motion
data, it automatically receives notifications with updated data, eliminating the need
to explicitly read it each time. This capability ensures low latency in data streaming,
providing a seamless gaming experience.
• GAP is the highest level of core BLE protocol, specifying the device role, modes,
discovery procedures, management of connection and security.
Based on the protocol stack above, BLE has two primary communication methods: broad-
casting and connections.
20
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:30==
Figure 2.7: GATT structure of Syncsense BLE motion sensor, containing motion service
and battery service.
Broadcasting involves sending data packets from a single device to multiple devices within
its listening range. This method is fast but not suitable for the transfer of private data.
It is primarily used when establishing connections, where a master device broadcasts
advertising packets to detect connectable slave devices.
A connection, on the other hand, is a private and secure data exchange between two de-
vices, with one acting as the master and the other as the slave. The process begins with
the slave broadcasting advertising packets. Meanwhile, the master, in discovery mode,
searches for reachable slaves and initiates a connection request. The slave acknowl-
edges the request, establishing the connection. Once connected, data can be securely
exchanged between the devices.

This section reviews the prior research completed by researchers in the DTU ReHyb team,

2.7.1
Design
Subsequently, we analyse the previews user experience-oriented theses.
The overall goals of the research was to improve the rehabilitation experience for the
three user groups with innovative and user-centred solutions. Although their research
focuses primarily on stroke patients’ rehabilitation, our research has a wider audience not
limited to stroke patients and is a wide usage for upper limb fitness. There are some
similarities between our main users, including the impairment of upper limb movement
and cognitive conditions. Furthermore, the other two user groups, physical therapist and
third-party caregivers, align with our research objectives, which are to help the primary
users exercise more efficiently. Overall, these research provided us instructive insights
and guidance.

writing by Jing Zhang (2022) [11] and Marco Puteo (2023) [14].

rehabilitation and explored how to enhance the user experience for patients. It defined

complex data visualisation. However, the concepts of patient management, scheduling,
and creating exercise plans for patients also validated as useful in this thesis.[13]

2.7.2
Implementation
The previous theses that this project builds upon also has a dashboard implementation,
where frameworks and tools like React, Next.js and MongoDB has been used [75], similar
to this project (Section 2.6.1). The setup of a full stack project is generally consistent,
though the choice of tools and frameworks varies based on factors such as familiarity,
necessity and performance. Often, familiarity is the primary factor, since projects are time
constrained, and using known frameworks can significantly speed up the development
process. The main purpose of the previous implementation was to act as a platform for
stroke patients, therapists and third party users. This corresponds somewhat with the full
stack purpose of this project. In contrast to this project, the previous thesis focused on
stroke patients which is different from our primary target group of this thesis, being people
with upper limb movement impairments, neither did the previous thesis involve any real
testing with any of its target groups. [75]
Additionally, this project features a technical difference, the dashboard is integrated with a
Unity application. This application, a game records that through external sensors, stores
the data according to specific users and then updates the user profiles in a MongoDB
database. Reversely, the changes from the dashboard is also reflected in the database
and the information shown in the Unity game, which means that the Unity application and
the dashboard web-based application would share one common server and database to
proceed the data transfer and keep the consistency of data display. Then, we decide to
separate the development between front-end and back-end server. However, through the
design phase, a lot of features that was implemented has been removed due to the aim
of a minimum viable product. Considering the scalability and reusability, we construct the
project from the scratch, at the same time, we still maintain a similar development phi-
losophy to the previous thesis, while using tools that make development easier, we must
also ensure that all functions are implemented in the dashboard and Unity application.
Although many features implemented in the previous thesis could be useful, they were
deemed unnecessary through feedback from stakeholders. And due to the significant
number of discarded features and the fact that access to the code was granted midway
through this project, the previous thesis has primarily served as a source of inspiration for
other parts of this project. Such as the database, where we used some of the attributes.
2.7.3
Game
In the domain of fitness and rehabilitation game, we review research from Irene, Sebastian
and Dumitru, Mark.
Irene designed and implemented a Virtual Reality(VR) system specifically for stroke reha-
bilitation. This system includes a ping pong VR game where players use a hand controller
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
23
==PAGE:33==
along with a 3D-printed racket to engage in the game. The game targets racket-based
activities like ping pong, making rehabilitation exercises more engaging and thus con-
tributing significantly to the field of VR interventions in stroke rehabilitation. [76]
Sebastian and Dumitru developed an innovative approach using exoskeletons equipped
with a Raspberry Pi, which communicates with a Unity application on a computer. Their
research explores the potential for remote rehabilitation, demonstrating that performing
exercises remotely using their system does not negatively impact the participants’ abil-
ity to complete the exercises. This highlights the feasibility and effectiveness of remote
rehabilitation interventions. [9]
Mark focused on developing a comprehensive framework to integrate various rehabilita-
tion exercises into games. His thesis proposed a universal abstraction layer that bridges
rehabilitation exercises and gameplay. This layer allows users to perform simple rehabili-
tation actions to engage with complex games. To validate this framework, Mark developed
a multiplayer virtual reality bowling game controlled by hand movements, demonstrating
that users could seamlessly engage with the game using the proposed system. [77]
These studies underscore the potential of games to facilitate rehabilitation exercises.
However, a notable challenge identified is the inherent contradiction between the simplic-
ity of repetitive rehabilitation exercises and the precise interaction mechanisms required
by conventional games. Previous research has relied heavily on external devices such as
hand controllers or exoskeletons to collect exercise data. These devices, while effective,
may not be suitable for all scenarios due to their bulkiness and the discomfort associated
with wearing external hardware.
While these studies focus on rehabilitation, their principles can also be applied to normal
fitness exercise. In our project, we aim to address the limitations commonly associated
with previous rehabilitation and fitness game. We incorporate user avatars and voice com-
panions during gameplay, integrating a vivid Human Digital Twin (HDT) into the games.
It can also reflect user past performance data in a racing game. This integration aims to
bridge the gap in fitness by studying how a vivid HDT can contribute to the effectiveness
of fitness exercises, enhancing both engagement and functional outcomes. By providing
real-time feedback and visual representation of progress, the HDT can motivate users
and make the exercising process more interactive and rewarding.
To better serve the concept of HDT, we need a more natural, comfortable, and accessible
way for users to engage with the game. To achieve this, we employ lightweight motion
sensors to capture inputs for VR games. This approach enables us to explore a wide
range of potential gameplay scenarios facilitated by motion sensors, focusing on creating
various interactions using simple motion data. By simplifying the interaction mechanics,
we can provide a more intuitive and immersive fitness experience, thereby avoiding the
drawbacks associated with cumbersome external devices and complex hand controllers.
By utilising accessible motion sensors and the concept of an engaging HDT, our project
seeks to improve the effectiveness of upper-limb exercise. This combination of accessi-
bility and engagement is designed to optimise fitness and rehabilitation outcomes, which
not only addresses the physical aspects of fitness but also considers the psychological
and motivational factors that can significantly impact user participation and success in
exercise programs.
