3
Methodology
This chapter outlines the methodologies employed throughout this thesis. The approach
includes a literature review, project management strategies, design processes, imple-
mentation methods, and evaluation techniques. Together, these methodologies form the
foundation for developing and testing the product.
3.1
Project Management
This project has used an agile project management method for the general workflow.
Contrary to the classical Waterfall methodology, where each stage of the project begins
only after the previous stage is completed, the agile method uses an iterative workflow.
Scrum, being an iterative method has been used for this project, it divides the project
into sections called sprints. Each sprint consists of several key components: planning,
review, and retrospectives. These overarching elements in a sprint aims to provide a
well-managed project through its whole process. [78]
In the Sprint Planning phases, the group had meetings to define the tasks and goals for
the upcoming sprint (Appendix A). This involved creating a sprint backlog, which includes
prioritised tasks selected from the product backlog. The product backlog lists all features,
functions, and fixes that needed to be addressed [78], the first backlog was primarily
derived from previous theses (Section 2.7), which worked with a similar product and re-
quirements from the product owner, including the professor and external partners. It was
therefore clear that those theses would formed the foundation for the prototype.
Throughout the sprint, daily scrums were held where each member briefly summarised
what had been done in the past 24 hours, discussing any issues, and bringing up relevant
questions to align the groups knowledge and progress. These meetings ensured contin-
uous communication and immediate problem-solving if any appeared [78].
At the end of each sprint, a Sprint Review meeting was held to inspect and change the
product backlog based on feedback which was primarily a weekly meeting with the super-
visor. But other stakeholders also participated occasionally to provide input and ensured
that the development aligned with their vision. Following the sprint review, a sprint retro-
spective was held to reflect on the sprint and identify areas for improvement. The group
discussed what went well, what did not, and how the processes should be changed to
avoid previous mistakes for future sprints.
A sprint backlog, was derived from the product backlog during each of the sprint planning
meetings, it contains the tasks that the group completes during the sprints. It then results
in a focused list of work items that help guide the group into the next sprint and how it
should be planned. [78]
By utilising Scrum, the iterative process allowed the group to quickly identify unnecessary
risks, and improving the overall process. This approach ensured continuous improve-
ment, adaptability, and effective collaboration between group members, leading to a well-
structured and efficiently managed thesis. The communication through daily scrums and
regular reviews ensured that all team members were aligned and that issues was instantly
addressed. The flexibility and responsive element of Scrum allow for adjustments based
on feedback from each sprint, ensuring that the project remains aligned with stakeholders
and users needs. [78]
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
25
==PAGE:35==
Figure 3.1: A visual representation of how the scrum process functions through the en-
tirety of a project. By having the iterative process repeat itself until certain conditions are
met [79].
3.1.1
Minimum Viable Product
A minimum viable product (MVP) is a version of a product with just enough features to
be usable by early customers who can then provide feedback for future product develop-
ment [80]. The MVP allows innovators to save cost and time by focusing on a ’bare bones’
version of the product that is sufficient to demonstrate value to users. This approach ac-
celerates the validation of the product’s value and growth hypotheses through targeted
experiments, avoiding the unnecessary development of full feature sets initially. By priori-
tising essential features and measuring market traction early, developers can efficiently
validate their concepts and make informed decisions on further development. [81]
The work process of MVP includes identifying the problem, defining the core features, de-
veloping a version of the product that contains only the essential features to be tested, util-
ising the MVP in experiments to validate the value and features of the product, analysing
the results from actual use and using the insights gained to refine the product, iterating on
the design as necessary, and finally deciding whether to proceed with the current strategy
or not [81].
In our project, due to limited time for implementation and validation, the concept of MVP
is very important. By focusing on essential features and reducing unnecessary functions,
we can complete the system and perform user testing more efficiently.
3.1.2
Adaptive Software Development
Given the projects scale and the need for flexibility, Adaptive Software Development
(ASD) was chosen as the primary software development method. Its matches with the
scale of this project, which require high adaptability and rapid iterations, making it ideal
for smaller projects. This also corresponds well with the objective being to develop a
MVP (Section 3.1.1). ASD focuses on a dynamic and flexible approach to software de-
velopment, focusing on the continuous progression of both the product and the process.
Unlike typical waterfall methodologies, ASD similar to scrum (Section 3.1) is an iterative
agile method. [82]
The process begins with a planning phase, which involves setting up the objectives of the
project and creating a high-level plan. It is important that this plan is not set in stone, as it
should allow for the iterative process to continuously evolve. The initial requirement are
found, and the team outlines a general direction for the development process, keeping in
mind that changes will be made in the progress. The next phase is collaboration with both
the developing team, which works together in short iterative cycles to develop the product.
26
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:36==
But also with the continuous communication and cooperation with the stakeholders. The
final phase is learn. This phase is dedicated to reflecting on the process and outcomes.
After each iteration, the team reviews what was accomplished, gathering feedback, and
then identifies areas for improvement. Mistakes that have been made during the current
phase, is discussed to ensure the process is refined for the next cycle. This is to ensure
that the team adapts to changes quickly and effectively. [82]
By using ASD, the project benefits greatly from a highly adaptable and iterative approach,
that allows the team to quickly respond to changes and new information from stakehold-
ers. This method emphasises on team work, and iterative development, aligning well with
the projects goal of integrating different technological features into one platform. ASDs
agile method over a typical waterfall method makes it very suitable for a dynamic project
like this.
3.1.3
Game Design MDA framework
The Mechanics, Dynamics, and Aesthetics (MDA) framework[83] provides a structured
methodology for comprehending games by dividing them into three core components:
Mechanics, Dynamics, and Aesthetics. Mechanics is the rules and systems that gov-
ern gameplay. Dynamics refer to the behaviours and interactions that emerge during
gameplay. Aesthetics are the emotional responses players experience. This framework
facilitates the connection between game design, development, and research, offering a
clear method for analysing and crafting engaging game experiences.
The MDA framework addresses both the designer’s and player’s perspectives. From
the designer’s viewpoint, mechanics are seen as the foundation that generates dynamic
behaviours, which in turn lead to specific aesthetic experiences. Conversely, players
begin with aesthetics, experiencing the dynamics and mechanics through their interaction
with the game. This dual perspective enables a comprehensive understanding of how
modifications in one component can impact the overall game experience. By utilising
precise terms such as sensation, fantasy, and challenge to describe aesthetics, and by
comprehending how dynamics create these experiences, designers can refine mechanics
to enhance gameplay. Therefore, the MDA framework is an efficient tool for designing and
analysing games with a focus on both player experience and system behaviour.
Figure 3.2: MDA framework connects game design and gameplay. Mechanics is the rules
and systems that govern gameplay. Dynamics refer to the behaviours and interactions
that emerge during gameplay. Aesthetics are the emotional responses players experi-
ence.[83]
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
27
==PAGE:37==
3.2
Systematic Literature Review
In reviewing literature for the theoretical background (Section 2) a systematic literature re-
view was conducted using a snowballing approach, as outlined by Wohlin in ”Guidelines
for Snowballing in Systematic Literature Studies and a Replication in Software Engineer-
ing” [84]. The snowballing technique, involving both backward and forward snowballing,
both was used to identify and gather relevant literature on relevant topics for this thesis.
The initial step involved identifying a start set of relevant papers. This was achieved
through a targeted search using Google Scholar and Connected Papers1, focusing on key
terms and phrases relevant to the research questions. The selected start set consisted
of important and highly cited papers in the domain, ensuring a broad and comprehensive
foundation for the snowballing process. Backward snowballing was the first method ap-
plied. This involved examining the reference lists of the papers in the start set to identify
additional relevant studies. Each reference was assessed based on its title, publication
venue, and authorship. Papers deemed relevant were retrieved and further evaluated by
reading their abstracts and, when necessary, the full texts. Ideally, this iterative process
continues until no new relevant papers were found in the reference lists of the included
studies, but due to time limitations this was done until a satisfactory amount of papers
were collected. Following backward snowballing, forward snowballing was conducted.
This involved identifying papers that cited the studies included from the backward snow-
balling phase. The same search engines was used to track citations of these papers.
Each of the cited papers was evaluated for relevance using a similar approach to back-
ward snowballing examining the title, abstract, and context of the citation. Relevant cited
papers were then included in the literature review.
Throughout the snowballing process, inclusion and exclusion criteria were applied con-
sistently to ensure the relevance and quality of the selected literature. Inclusion criteria
focused on studies that were peer-reviewed, published in reputable journals or confer-
ences, and directly addressed the research questions. Exclusion criteria ruled out papers
that were not peer-reviewed or outside the scope of the research questions. For the final
set of included papers key information such as study objectives, methods, results, and
conclusions were systematically extracted and organised. This data was then used to
identify trends, and gaps in the existing literature [84].
By using the snowballing method for systematic literature review it has provided a ro-
bust foundation for collecting existing knowledge and identifying research questions and
requirements.
3.3
Design Process
3.3.1
Persona
Personas are fictional characters, which is created based upon the research in order to
represent the different user types that might use in service, product, or brand in a simi-
lar way. Creating personas helps researchers to understand users’ needs, experiences,
behaviours and goals. [86]
Persona can be used from multiple perspectives [87]. In this project, we create personas
from the goal-directed perspective. The objective of a goal-directed persona is to exam-
ine the process and workflow that users would prefer to utilise to achieve their goals in
interacting with a product or service. There is an implicit assumption that researchers
have already done enough user research to recognise that their product has value to the
user and that by examining their goals, teams can bring their requirements to life. [86][88]
1A tool to find relevant papers on a specific topic, showing how the papers are connected through citations
together [85].

To create persona profiles, detailed descriptions need to be considered, include demo-
graphic information (age, gender, occupation), user behaviours, goals, needs, frustra-
tions, and motivations. Add fictional personal details to make the personas more realistic
and relatable. And these profiles help in understanding the users deeply and creating
empathy among the members. [86]
3.3.2
Ability Prompt Cards
Designing for people with permanent disabilities or extreme scenarios can seem like a
significant constraint, but can in fact result in solutions that can benefit a much larger
number of people. The Ability Prompt Cards designed by DTU Skylab provide examples of
disabilities, and are not to be seen as a comprehensive overview, but rather as inspiration
for designers to use to challenge the solutions. [89]
The Ability Prompt Cards can be used for different processes of product development.
In ideation process, for example, during brainstorming, the cards can help identify poten-
tial user abilities affected by the design, ensuring that ideas are inclusive from the start.
While creating a prototype, it can be refer to check if the design is accessible to users with
limited mobility or cognitive impairments. This iterative process helps refine prototypes to
be more inclusive.[89, 90] In our project, we used the cards as a presenting tool to stake-
holders, including therapists, researchers, and developers in related areas, to ensure that
we can cover all potential disability and scenarios. That gave us a clear picture of users’
abilities when we continued with the subsequent design and development.
Figure 3.3: The overview of ability prompt cards [89]
3.3.3
Concept
A concept represents the initial idea or vision of a product, which encompasses its ex-
pected functionalities, elements, and user interactions. Sproll et al. indicated states that
a concept idea can already exhibit the potentials of a positive UX [91]. Concept develop-
ment is the bridge between empathetic understanding and tangible innovation. It’s where
the insights gleaned from deep dives into user experiences, needs, and challenges are
synthesised into viable ideas that have the potential to evolve into solutions. [92]
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
29
==PAGE:39==
In our project, we use concepts to identify and define the key elements and functionalities
to provide clear goals for the subsequent prototyping stage.
3.3.4
Task Analysis
Task Analysis refers to the broad practice of learning about how users work to achieve
their goals. A task refers to any activity that is usually observable and has a start and
an end point. It suggest that the analysis is of just one task, task analysis may address
multiple tasks, all in service of the same goal. Task analysis is crucial for user experience,
because a design that solves the wrong problem that does not support users’ tasks will
fail, no matter how good its UI. [93]
The task-analysis process can be separated as two stages:
Stage 1: Gather information on goals and tasks by observing and speaking with users
and/or subject-matter experts. [94, 93]
Stage 2: Analyse the tasks performed to achieve goals to understand the overall number
of tasks and subtasks, their sequence, their hierarchy, and their complexity. The analyst
typically produces diagrams to document this analysis. [95, 93]
We used this method to specify users requirements, ensuring the product solve real prob-
lems for users.
3.3.5
Prototyping
Prototyping is an experimental process where design teams implement ideas into tangible
forms from paper to digital. Teams build prototypes of varying degrees of fidelity to capture
design concepts and test on users. Prototypes can refine and validate the designs so
teams can release the right products. [96]
Prototyping can vary in fidelity, from low-fidelity sketches to high-fidelity interactive mod-
els. Low-Fidelity Prototypes are simple sketches or wireframes that focus on basic layout
and functionality. High-Fidelity Prototypes are detailed and interactive models that closely
resemble the final product. These are used to test specific functionalities and user inter-
actions. [97]
While low-fidelity prototypes are useful for making changes and test new iterations at the
early design stage, the feature of lacking of realism making users have a hard time giving
feedback [96]. So during the design process, we mainly used high-fidelity prototype to
show the accurate functions to stakeholders, accelerating the development process and
get comprehensive feedback.
3.4
User Experience Assessments
3.4.1
Technology Acceptance
Technology acceptance(TAM) is an theoretical model to explain and predict user be-
haviour when use a new technology. It is rooted in two primary psychosocial theories:
the Theory of Reasoned Action (TRA) by Fishbein and Ajzen (1975) and the Theory of
Planned Behaviour (TPB) by Ajzen (1991). It divides the external variables that could
influence the users’ decision about how and when they will use the technology into two
different parts, the first one is to identify the degree of the users believes that this tech-
nology is useful for them(Perceived usefulness), and the other is the degree of the users
believes that this system is easy for them to use(Perceived ease-of-use). After having
clarified those two questions, the users’ attitudes are explicit. Then, the researchers are
able to dive into the users’ attitudes to figure out their intentions related to if they would
like to use the technology or not and their acceptance. Finally, the researchers could
30
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:40==
Figure 3.4: Technology acceptance model
[98]
observe the actual use of the new technology to see the difference between theory and
actual situation3.4. [98]
TAM provides a robust framework to predict and understand user acceptance and usage
behaviour towards new technologies. And it offers a simple and concise model with few
variables that are easy to measure and analyse, if there is any potential variables that
could affect the measure result, we could integrate them with this model easily to make
the result more precise. For our system, the user group is the people with upper-limb
impairments and some of them have the cognitive impairment, and they must use our
system under the guidance of their therapists, thus we could not assume that they feel
the same way as normal people. There might be some barriers for them to use the system,
because what they consistently do is physical rehabilitation￿which is definitely different
from how our system process rehabilitation.
In order to make our design principles align with their goals perfectly, we observe their
reactions when playing our games based on the variables generated by using TAM. In
this way, we could identify the difficulties faces by them, the factors they like or dislike
in the game, also gather different insights from their perspectives. Having compared the
result generated by the TAM to the result of actual usage of the system, it is easy for us
to improve iterate our system design, to make our system more accessible and feasible.
[99]
During our testing, we figured out the physical and mental situations of our users firstly,
then designed several variables and questions we want to measure based on the targeted
user group. Having figure out those theoretical factors, we prepared various quantitative
questions, questionnaires and the interview to assess perceived usefulness, perceived
ease of use, and intention to use of the users. Finally, we analysed the data collected in
the testing, and interpret the analysed result based on our research questions.
3.4.2
UX Surveys
UX Surveys is a tool to gather information about users’ feelings, thoughts, and behaviours
related to UX design, product, or service. It is a part of the broader field of usability
surveys. And it focus on understanding how users interact with a system to create a
UCD. [100]
We mainly employed close-ended questions in the surveys. Enable users to pick from
predefined options like checkboxes or scales. This way are suitable for gathering data.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
31
==PAGE:41==
The answers provide actionable data, like customer preferences or standard problems.
Moreover users don’t need to type out their thoughts. They select an option that best
describes their feelings. [100]
3.4.3
Likert Scale
Likert scale is a set of statements offered for a real or hypothetical situation under study.
Participants are asked to show their level of agreement (from strongly disagree to strongly
agree) with the given statement on a metric scale.[101] For the range of options, A five
point scale was employed in our study, to allow an individual to express how much they
agree or disagree with a particular statement. It provides five possible answers to a state-
ment or question that allows respondents to indicate their positive-to-negative strength of
agreement or strength of feeling regarding the question or statement.[102]
We utilised the Likert scale for the questions provided in the surveys, gathering feedback
about user preferences and obtain intuitive and assessable results.
3.4.4
Task Scenarios for Usability Testing
Task scenarios for usability testing need to provide context so users engage with the in-
terface and pretend to perform business or personal tasks. Engaging participants by task
scenarios that are realistic, encourage an action. Effective task scenarios are actionable
and relevant, ensuring meaningful feedback. [103] It helps to gather valuable feedback,
especially for specific hypothetical questions, such as the assumptions in the usage sce-
nario.
3.4.5
User interview
User interview is A research method where the interviewer asks participants questions
about a topic, listens to their responses, and follows up with further questions to learn
more. Here are some of the many things we can learn by interviewing users: What users’
experiences were like, what was memorable, and why; Users’ pain points during an ex-
perience; How users think or feel about a topic, event, or experience; What users care
about; Users’ mental models; Users’ motivations, aspirations, and desires. [104] In our
project, we primarily use interview to gather feedback during user testing sessions.
3.5
Data Streaming From Sensors to VR Game
3.5.1
Motion Sensor
Data streaming is integral to the functionality of our product, as the VR gameplay relies
on precise and real-time motion data. Additionally, for the embodiment of HDT, user per-
formance data, essentially motion data, is indispensable. Effective handling of sensor
connections and data processing is paramount to ensuring the seamless operation and
immersive experience of our VR system. Accurate data streaming not only enhances
gameplay but also plays a crucial role in creating an effective and responsive HDT.
To achieve this, we utilise BLE communication technology, which offers a robust, energy-
efficient, and low-latency solution for transmitting motion data from sensors to VR head-
sets. This technology ensures that the motion data, which forms the core input for our VR
games, is transmitted in real-time, facilitating smooth and responsive gameplay.
In the following sections, we provide an overview of how data is represented and pro-
cessed within the game environment, ensuring that motion data from sensors is accurately
translated into game inputs.
The motion sensors we use are provided by Syncsense, are equipped with an IMU com-
prising accelerometer and gyroscope, and BLE communication. It can transmit data via
BLE at a frequency of 30 Hz, enabling real-time, instant control.
32
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:42==
Among the various VR headsets reviewed in Section 2.6.6.2, our implementation utilises
PICO NEO 3 Pro VR headsets. First of all, these headsets are equipped with Bluetooth 5.1
connectivity and operate on the Android 10 platform. Bluetooth 5.1 connectivity ensures
robust and reliable communication between the VR headsets and the Syncsense motion
sensors, which is essential for accurate data collection and user interaction during the
exercises. Besides, the PICO NEO 3 Pro headset employs inside-out tracking technology,
making it particularly suitable for our use case where users engage in exercise routines
within a confined space, without moving around the area. This tracking method obviates
the need for external tracking hardware, facilitating a more convenient and rapid setup
process for the game. Additionally, the compatibility of Syncsense sensors with the PICO
NEO 3 Pro headsets is a crucial factor, given that these sensors are primarily designed
to target this particular model of VR headsets.
In summary, the integration of Syncsense motion sensors with the PICO NEO 3 Pro VR
headsets represents a well-considered choice, leveraging the strengths of both technolo-
gies to deliver an effective solution for fitness exercise conducted in a VR environment.
The specific design and capabilities of these devices align well with our requirements,
ensuring compatibility, ease of use, and optimal performance.
For simplicity, we will use the terms headset or VR headset to refer to the PICO NEO 3
Pro VR headset, and sensor or motion sensor to refer to the Syncsense sensor.
3.5.2
BLE Communication with VR Game
In this section, we address the BLE communication between motion sensors and the VR
game developed using the Unity engine.
The game runs on a PICO NEO 3 Pro VR headset, which is an Android Platform. Unity
primary scripting language is C#, but it also allow using other Java source code as an
external plugin to achieve some features in the game. In our implementation, we employ
a Java source plugin from Syncsense within the Unity script to utilise Android BLE APIs for
managing BLE connections. This process involves subscribing to a GATT characteristic in
a BLE device, using the device name or Medium Access Control (MAC) address, service
UUID, and characteristic UUID to locate the required data.

3.5.3
Sensor Data Preprocessing
We defined the data structure by C# in Unity, where RawDataReceived is the raw data re-
ceived from the Java plugin without any processing. Its child class SensorDataReceived,
is the processed data, which parses the raw data from RawDataReceived to motion data.
DeviceAddress field is the MAC Address of the sensor.
Figure 3.5: Data structure in Unity script. RawDataReceived is the raw data from the
Java plugin without any processing. SensorDataReceived is the processed data from
RawDataReceived, containing the fields for motion data. It’s also a child class of Raw-
DataReceived.
We can have a closer look at the raw data received by the headset store in the 25-byte
array RawDataReceived.data, where index occupies 1 byte, and the data from each axis
of accelerometer and gyroscope occupies 4 bytes, coming up with 25 bytes in total. We
parse the raw data to motion data based on this structure.
Figure 3.6: Raw data received by VR headset. 25 bytes in total, 1 byte for index from 0
to 127, 4 bytes for accelerometer X, Y, Z axis, 4 bytes for gyroscope X, Y, Z axis
The motion data containing complete acceleration and rotation information facilitates game
development of different genres, while it need be processed into a value that is interpreted
in the context of that game, not the context of motion movement. For example, for a
wheelchair game, the input data can be interpreted as left-wheel force and right-wheel
force. Therefore, another layer of encapsulation is introduced, it should be able to trans-
form the motion data of SensorDataReceived class to game input. We can achieve this
simply by adding a event handler inside the callback function OnDataReceived mentioned
above.
1

Then we can manage OnSensorDataReceivedEvent for subscribing the corresponding

To summarize, when new sensor data is received by the VR headset, it invokes the On-
CharacteristicChanged method in the Java code. This method subsequently triggers the
SyncsenseSensorManager.OnDataReceived method in the Unity script, passing along
the raw data. Within this Unity method, the raw sensor data is converted into motion
data. Following this conversion, the OnSensorDataReceivedEvent method is invoked
to process the motion data into various forms of game input data using corresponding
processing algorithms.
This data processing workflow is designed to ensure accurate and responsive control
within the VR environment. The VR game includes three distinct target exercises and
four mini games, each requiring a unique control method and corresponding data pro-
cessing technique. These exercises and their specific data processing methodologies
will be elaborated upon in section 4. By utilising these techniques, our system ensures
a seamless integration of sensor data into the VR game, enhancing the effectiveness of
the fitness process.

4
System
4.1
UX Design
In this section, we present the UX design processes. Our goal is to create an intuitive and
accessible solution to meet the needs of our target user groups. Firstly, we defined the
target user groups, created personas to represent them, and created concepts to show
the functionality’s direction. Following this, we conducted a task analysis to detail the
interactions that our users will have with the system. Additionally, we created the prototype
to provide a seamless user experience. Iterating it before finalising the implementation
stage, to ensure it can move to the next stage as a MVP and guarantee the efficiency of
the implementation.
It is worth mentioning that the game components in the dashboard are based on the func-
tionality of specific devices we have, including hand cycles, dumbbells, and wheelchairs.
The details of these games are introduced in the following Game Platform section. This
section focuses mainly on the design of the dashboards for both the web and Game plat-
forms.
4.1.1
Target User Groups
The subsequent part discusses the identified user groups, the primary users, physical
therapists, and third-party caregivers. Primary users are people with upper-limb impair-
ment and need to exercise upper-limbs. Therapists assist primary users with their daily ex-
ercise routines and assist. Third-party caregivers are typically family members or guardians
of the primary users. They do not have a sufficient amount of professional clinical knowl-
edge, but they are capable of providing support to primary users in their daily lives.
4.1.1.1
Ability Prompt Cards
We utilised Ability Prompt Cards to introduce the deficits in the possible physical or psy-
chological situations of our primary users. It aims to provide a clear understanding of the
problems and situations our users might face.
As we mentioned in Section 2.4, upper-limb movement impairment can be caused by a
number of conditions and incidents. Based on the research and discussions with thera-
pists and professionals, we created and iterated the table of ability prompt cards (Figure
4.1).
Figure 4.1: Table of ability prompt cards of the primary users.
38
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:48==
The primary conditions of the primary users are varied. In terms of touch, they might have
problems using one arm. The arm injury can be temporary or permanent. For movement,
some of them rely on a wheelchair, which requires them to strengthen their upper limbs
in order to enhance their quality of life. In holding, tremors can be a significant issue. And
sports injuries might cause difficulties.
As for potential conditions, while not as common, they also affect our users. In under-
standing, dyslexia is a potential problem for a part of users. Thinking, they might deal
with dementia, and stress can be a temporary condition when they first come into touch
with new technologies and equipment. Lastly, when new devices or exercise routines
have a steep learning curve, emotions like grief and anger are a potential challenge.
Additionally, according to the universal design principles (Section 2.5.2), we do not include
users who cannot perceive visual information or those with cognitive abilities that are
insufficient to understand and navigate the interface.
We considered these features in design and implementation, as well as in the scheduling
of user testing, ensuring that the system is comprehensive and able to be used by the
targeted user group, making it universal and inclusive for the primary users.
4.1.1.2
Persona
According to the prior insights and analysis from previews research, we created the per-
sonas for the three user groups. These personas and user needs were initially derived
from research focused on upper limb fitness and rehabilitation systems (Section 2.7).
These insights are valuable for our project on upper limb fitness, as both domains address
the exercise needs of individuals with upper limb impairments. Users in both groups have
similar challenges and goals in maintaining and improving their physical condition.
The primary user persona (Figure 4.2) shows that the user faces challenges with his
fitness routine. The reasons are that traditional fitness exercises are often monotonous,
and attending frequent in-person therapy sessions is inconvenient. He struggled with
maintaining motivation when without supervision. He seeks solutions that allow for more
engaging and enjoyable workouts that enhance his upper limb strength.
Figure 4.2: Persona of the primary user
The therapist persona (Figure 4.3) shows that the therapist faces challenges in efficiently
managing patient care. The main issues are difficulties in keeping track of each patient’s
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
39
==PAGE:49==
progress on busy days and handling large amounts of patient data by hand. These chal-
lenges hinder the therapist’s ability to ensure that patients perform their exercises cor-
rectly and effectively. The therapist wishes for tools that simplify the monitoring of patient
progress and integrate data management without needing frequent manual updates, aim-
ing to make patient management and therapy sessions more organised and effective.
Figure 4.3: Persona of the therapist
The third-party caregiver persona (Figure 4.4) presents that the user faces challenges
in assisting his brother with exercise routines. The primary difficulties are finding ways
to keep his brother motivated and using tools that his brother can easily use despite his
impairments. He wants to understand better the fitness exercise process to provide ef-
fective support. He wants tools or technologies that enhance his brother’s autonomy in
engaging in activities he enjoys, and that are easy to use.
Figure 4.4: Persona of the third-party caregiver
4.1.2
Concept
From the personas, we can see the key wishes and needs of each user group. To meet
these needs and align with the technology and tools available in the group, we set the
following concepts.

Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:50==
• Primary users: An easy-to-use tool that provides supervision, engaging games and
guidance to make the fitness exercise more motivating.
• Therapists: A digital system that monitors primary users’ exercise process and phys-
ical condition, and manages their exercise routines.
• Third-party caregivers: a digital platform that enables them to understand the ex-
ercise routines of the primary users, giving them opportunities to be involved in the
fitness process.
4.1.3
Task Analysis
In this section, we present the detailed task analysis about how users interact with the
system, to understand the needs of primary users, therapists, and third-party caregivers
in the use case. It helped us to sort out the process to generate useful insights for proto-
typing, implementing and user testing process design.
4.1.3.1
Pre-therapy Session Tasks
Figure 4.5: Pre-therapy Session Task Analysis Diagram
As shown in Figure 4.5, before starting therapy sessions, primary users need to familiarise
themselves with the guidelines and user guide for the headset and application. They log
in using the account provided by the therapist and check the General Data Protection
Regulation (GDPR) consent form. Therapists begin by collecting basic information about
the patient, acquiring the headset, and signing up in the system. They read the guidelines
for both the games and the patients, create an account for the patient, and familiarise
themselves with the game and avatar. Third-party caregivers sign up on the dashboard,
also need to agree to the GDPR consent form, and check the same guidelines. They
ensure primary users are aware of processes and can provide them with support. For the
system and support actions, The group behind the system should provide documents of
guidance and GDPR. And The system creates accounts for users. These tasks ensure
the system operates smoothly and complies with relevant regulations.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
41
==PAGE:51==
4.1.3.2
During-therapy Session Tasks
As shown in Figure 4.6, during therapy sessions, primary users engage in tasks like set-
ting their avatar, wearing the headset, and starting the game. They receive guidance
and feedback from an avatar, which helps maintain motivation and ensures correct ex-
ercise performance. They will learn how to use the system at the first therapy session
with the system and be able to use it more independently in the future. Therapists play
a crucial role by providing instructions, answering questions regarding privacy and data,
introducing the headset, and helping with the setup. They monitor the patient’s exercise
progress, provide feedback, and adjust the fitness plan as needed. For third-party care-
givers, their participation in this session is optional, depending on whether the primary
user can complete the session independently. If they attend, they will assist by helping
to create an avatar, suggesting suitable games, and ensuring the patient is comfortable
with the equipment. They can learn to use the live view function during the session. And
helping adjust the exercise plan. The system’s role includes setting avatars, collecting
user data, providing real-time data transfer, and generating feedback for users. The sys-
tem ensures that all interactions are tracked and analysed to improve the overall therapy
experience.
Figure 4.6: During-therapy Session Task Analysis Diagram
4.1.3.3
Post-therapy Session Tasks
After the therapy sessions, tasks are shown in Figure 4.7. In the ideal situation, primary
users can exercise without the presence of the therapist. In another situation, users can
exercise more independently while still at the location of the therapist, allowing therapists
to have more flexible time without constant supervision. The primary user can start by
checking their avatar and reviewing feedback from the system. They then choose the ap-
propriate equipment and select the desired game. After wearing the headset and adjust-
ing the difficulty level, they begin the exercise. During the exercise, they receive real-time
feedback. Once finished, they check their avatar on the dashboard again and can proceed
to play more games if needed. Therapists analyse the data collected during this session,
provide feedback and update the fitness plans if it is needed. They also document any
42
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:52==
issues or suggestions for system improvement to ensure the therapy process remains
efficient and effective. Third-party caregivers can review the feedback provided by the
system and therapist, help the primary users adjust their exercise routines, and ensure
that the users remain motivated. The system compiles all user data, generates compre-
hensive reports, and provides insights for both therapists and caregivers. It ensures that
the feedback loop is complete, helping all stakeholders stay informed and engaged.
Figure 4.7: Post-therapy Session Task Analysis Diagram
4.1.4
Prototype
This section describes the user interface (UI) functionalities of our prototype. The UI
is based on our concepts. Some elements are reused and adapted from previous re-
search to fit our needs. We adopted the concept of a minimum viable product aiming
to meet user needs with a streamlined set of functionalities and components. Following
WebAIM’s guidelines (Section 2.5.2.1), we aimed to create the prototype using simplified
navigation, clear instructions, minimal distractions, and recognisable visual elements to
address problem-solving, attention, and visual comprehension deficits, ensuring acces-
sibility for users with mild cognitive disability [41]. We explores colour schemes, the key
features, and the overall layout of the interface.
Colour scheme
The colour scheme (Figure 4.8) ensures the UI’s readability and pro-
vides visual coherence. We use a blue as the main colour and a lighter blue as the sec-
ondary colour. The orange is used as the accent colour, highlighting important elements
such as buttons. The yellow is also used for tasks waiting to start, the green indicates
completed tasks and positive status, and the red colour indicates situations where users
need to pay attention, such as overdue tasks and the delete button.
Figure 4.8: Colour scheme of UI
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
43
==PAGE:53==
4.1.4.1
Primary Users Platform
The subsequent discussion describes the UI designed for primary users. It covers how
they can check their information, access games, and interaction details.
The primary users use VR headsets to access the system, check their information, and
play games. Initially, we conceived their dashboard as a web platform, similar to previous
research conducted by the Rehyb team. However, in our project, users utilise VR head-
sets to play games, needing an interface that allows them to enter games through the VR
system. This led us to redirect our design and create a VR-compatible interface in the
Unity platform, ensuring seamless integration of game access and information checking.
Figure 4.9: The UI of primary users: Main page
Main page
The main page UI (Figure 4.9) includes the user’s name, profile image, total
exercise days, credits, and title at the top. Users collect credits based on their real game
time, allowing them to upgrade their titles to higher levels, which motivates them. Below
this, there are two buttons for measuring their range of motions and pairing the sensor
on the exercise devices. The thumbs-up component displays feedback from their contact
persons, such as third-party caregivers and therapists, to provide motivation and a sense
of support. In the centre of the page is the user’s personalised avatar, which greets them
with customised messages. It is worth mentioning that the hologram effect that blocks the
avatar’s legs is intended to reduce users’ attention to the lower limbs and make them focus
on the upper limbs. It also takes into account their emotions, because a part of primary
users cannot stand like the avatar. The hologram effect can weaken this information.
This hologram effect is also used in the feedback system of therapists and third-party
caregivers. On the right side, a Play Games button directs users to the games page,
where they can select games to play.
Game selection page
The game selection page (Figure B.1, Appendix B) is accessed
via the ‘Play Games’ button on the main page. This page displays the tasks that the thera-
pists assign in a gameplay way and details of the tasks, with details including the number
of sets, the estimated finishing time for all sets, the difficulty level, and the credits that can
be earned. It also specifies the deadline for completing the exercise and categorises the
games as in process, done, or awaiting start. At the top of the page, users can select the
devices to filter the games by choosing from different devices tabs: wheelchair, dumbbell,
and hand cycle. Below the tasks, all games are allowed to be chosen even if they are
not the tasks, which also serves as a potential future extension to accommodate more
games.
44
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout

Virtual Therapist
For the range of motion measuring page and pairing for the sensor
page, we introduced a virtual therapist character to guide users through the setup pro-
cess more efficiently. This character was designed to be a user-friendly image to provide
a sense of support, making the system feel more engaging. The detailed flow and me-
chanics of pairing and measuring ROM are described in the following Game Platform
section. For the full view of the pages, please refer to Figure B.2 of Appendix B.
4.1.4.2
Therapists Dashboard
This part describes the therapist’s feedback system UI. We use clients to represent the
primary users for therapists. The UI includes the client list, adding clients and the client
details page. Through this system, therapists can manage client details, add and edit
tasks, and check the task progress and physical status of their clients.
Client list page
The client list page (Figure B.3, Appendix B) is the first page therapists
see upon signing into the system. It includes the therapist’s name and ID. On the top,
there is a search bar for searching clients’ names. The default order is to follow the time
of adding clients, and they can order the list by name. The two main buttons on the right
side, one for adding new clients and another for archiving clients who are no longer under
their therapy sessions. The menu bar on the left is reserved for future function expansions.
The client list displays information about each client, including their name, current task
count, total exercise hours, and exercise hours for the current week. Therapists can click
the “Details” button to view more information or click the “Manage Tasks” button to as-
sign and manage their exercise tasks. Additionally, therapists can click the “Thumbs-Up”
button to send encouragement, which will appear in the client’s interface. The “Archive”
button allows therapists to move a client to the archived client list.
Adding client page
The Adding Clients page allows therapists to input comprehensive
information for new clients. Therapists need to fill in sections for basic information, in-
cluding the client’s first name, last name, birth date, phone number, email, and biological
sex. They can also create an avatar for the client, which is a component. The recovery
plan section focuses mainly on the upper limbs and includes details about the type of
movement impairment, the dominant arm, and additional notes about the client’s physical
condition. Additionally, therapists also need to enter information for the contact person,
including their name, email, and phone number. For the full view of this page, please refer
to Figure B.4 of Appendix B.
Client details page
The page (Figure 4.10) allows therapists to review comprehensive
information about their clients. This includes basic information, which was previously
added at creation. It also provides detailed task information, including the tasks assigned
to the client, their completion rate, and the status of each task (done, in process, await-
ing start, and overdue). Additionally, therapists can monitor the client’s range of motion
(ROM), showing the degrees to which the client’s arms can be kept straight and lifted to
know the physical condition of their upper limbs.
Tasks management page
This page (Figure 4.11) can be entered from the “Manage
tasks” button in the client list page or the “Manage” button in the task component of the
client details page. The page allows therapists to add and manage exercise tasks for their
clients. On this page, therapists can select games, specify the number of sets, and set
the task period. In the task list, they can check the status of all tasks. The tasks that have
expired are also shown below.

4.1.4.3
Third-party Caregivers Dashboard
Next, we describes the UI designed for third-party caregivers. Their dashboard contains
features including an exercise live entrance, thumbs-up functionality, and access to de-
tailed information about the exercise process, tasks, and physical condition of the primary
users. This interface is designed for encouraging and monitoring the fitness progress of
the primary users.
Main Page
The main page (Figure 4.12) includes several key components. In the top left
corner, there’s basic information about the third-party caregiver, including their name and
contact details for the therapist. An “Exercise Live” button allows third-party caregivers to
watch the primary users play games live, provided they are on the same internet network.
On the right, the thumbs-up count section shows the primary user’s name, the number of
days and hours they have exercised, and a “Give Thumbs Up” button for sending encour-
agement.
Below these sections, the tasks component displays three current tasks. Clicking “More”
will open to a detailed tasks popup window (Figure 4.12) showing all current tasks. When
they hover over the game images in the task component, the gameplay demo videos help
them understand the player’s perspective in games.

4.1.5
Prototype Iteration
This section outlines the process of iterating the prototype of our UI before finalising the
implementation work. Our goal was to ensure we achieved a minimum viable product
while aligning with the feasibility of implementing all features within our time constraints.
To do this, we conducted several meetings with researchers in related fields, therapists
involved in user testing, and other professionals in the field. We presented the concepts of
the system and UI during the meetings and then recorded and discussed their feedback.
We also conducted an internal assessment meeting to estimate the implementation time
for each feature. The discussions and assessment helped refine our design and address
practical concerns. After that, we iterated some UI.
4.1.5.1
Feedback
In a meeting with the CEO and a senior developer of Syncsense, a Danish company
specialising in innovative VR-rehabilitation solutions, we received crucial feedback. They
pointed out that the prototype was more complex than necessary for an MVP. Additionally,
In a discussion session with our supervisor and co-supervisor, they mentioned that the
design of the incentive system in primary users platform, including titles and credits, needs
to be clarified and aligned with the core goals of the project.
In a meeting with three professional physical therapists, they provided valuable insights
into the system. For the primary users platform, they indicated that students would be
motivated and excited to see their avatars if they were created by themselves. However,
they did not find the concept of the virtual therapist or avatar of therapists necessary. They
mentioned that the therapist’s image in the system is irrelevant to the primary users.
Furthermore, based on the group assessment of time and implementation complexity, we
cut and simplified some features without harming core functionalities.
4.1.5.2
Iteration Results
This section describes the iteration results of the prototype according to the feedback and
group consideration.
Primary users: Main page
After considering the feedback from stakeholders, we de-
cided to remove credits to earn titles in the system of the primary users. The updated UI
is in (Figure 4.13).

4.2.1
Application Architecture
Our system integrates a comprehensive dashboard within a Unity game, facilitating seam-
less interactions among a web-based application, the Unity game, and a database. This
section details the communication architecture between these different platforms, as illus-
trated in Figure 4.15. The web dashboard comprises a front-end application and a server.
The Unity game connects with sensors to process data, which is then utilised within the
Unity project and deployed on VR glasses.
48
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:58==
Figure 4.15: HDT Application Architecture
To ensure data consistency across platforms, both the Unity game and the dashboard
access a centralised database for data retrieval. The subsequent sections will provide
a detailed account of the implementation of our system. By maintaining a unified data
source, we ensure that the information displayed remains consistent, irrespective of the
platform. This approach simplifies data management and integrity across the system.
4.2.2
Functional Requirements Analysis
Functional requirements analysis was considered during design phase, when we have
analysed plenty of similar applications for the feedback system. The most important ref-
erence is the last version of our product that we have talked about in the Section ??.
As the basis of those literature reviews3.2 and the prior theses analysis, we have nar-
rowed down the user requirements and system functions. After having clarified the func-
tional requirements, we then made the models and business services in the back-end
to implement those functional requirements technically. The functional requirements and
corresponding microservices have been organised in the table 4.1. [105]
4.2.3
User Case Design
There are three roles in our actors, which is therapist, patient and caregivers, and only the
therapist and caregivers needs to get access to the dashboard. The use case design has
been implemented in Figure 4.16 to show the interactions between users and our web
dashboard.
4.2.4
Database Design
When designing the database using MongoDB, our system builds upon the database
design of the previous system (Section 2.7) while retaining certain available data and
fields. To align with our system design, our database is design specifically for storing the
users’ personal information corresponding to HDT embodiment requirements. Given the
relatively scattered nature of the data and the absence of strong connections among all
the data points, our focus shifts towards a document-oriented approach as suggested by

MongoDB. As a result, our system primarily revolves around a unified database structure,
grouping data into five main collections: ”Therapist Profile”, ”Patient Profile”, ”Care-
givers Profile”, Game Category, and ”Sign-up Users”.
In ”Patient Profile” collections, we have the following attributes that directly related to
HDT. This database design support the HDT embodiment in both web dashboard and VR
games, and can be used for both normal fitness exercise as well as rehabilitation exercise.
• (string) name. Name of the user.
• (string) impaired. The impairment description.
• (string) dominant arm. The available arms.
• (string) gender. Male or female.
• (int) thumbs. The count of thumb-up this user receive.
• (string) avatar. The Uniform Resource Locator(URL) for the avatar.
• (double) total exercise hours. Hours this user has been exercising in total.
• (double) weekly exercise hours. Hours this user has been exercising in current
week.
When conducting queries across different collections in a NoSQL database, we store
the necessary field value within specific documents in other collections. This approach
is chosen because NoSQL databases often do not enforce strict relationships between
collections.

4.2.4.1
Data Modelling
Data modelling involves determining the types of data we need access to, including enti-
ties, attributes, and relationships between each document. Considering the functionalities
of our system, we identified the attributes that should be stored in each document. For
example, when designing documents for therapists and caregivers, which share similar
functions and require minimal data storage, we opted for a simple configuration. These
documents contain basic key-value pairs such as name, patients’ id array, ID, and email,
minimising memory usage by using string arrays instead of complicated embedded enti-
ties.
However, patient data is dynamic, reflecting their progress and performance in game ex-
ercises. To ensure accurate data display in both the game and dashboard, we embedded
multiple entities within patient documents to represents their personal data and maps to
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
51
==PAGE:61==
the information required by the HDT embodiment in the system. Although this approach
may consume more memory, it significantly enhances query speed as all relevant data is
stored within the same document. In contrast, for static documents like game categories
and sign-up users, we designed them to be read-only without any embedded objects.
4.2.4.2
Implementation in MongoDB Atlas
Having introduced the concepts of how we design the data model, the implementation
of the database design would be illustrated in this small section.We implemented the
database design in MongoDB Atlas with a combination of the JSON file, MongoDB Com-
pass and the Web interfaces.
JSON file
JavaScript Object Notation (JSON), is widely recognised as a lightweight
data-interchange format [107]. We choose JSON to represent our data due to its sim-
plicity in readability and writing, making it an ideal choice for our needs. Moreover, JSON
seamlessly integrates with MongoDB Atlas, allowing us to import data directly into our
database. Serving as our primary tool for constructing data model structures, JSON fa-
cilitated batch data copying and importing processes, streamlining our workflow and en-
hancing efficiency. The example of the JSON file for the Sign-up User would show in the
below:
{
"name": "caregiver_1",
"email": "caregiver_1@test.com",
"password": "1111",
"status": "offline",
"role": "Caregiver"
}
MongoDB Compass
MongoDB Compass serves as a user-friendly graphical interface
for managing database operations. Offering a range of functions, it enables visual explo-
ration and processing of data within the database.
Upon establishing a connection to MongoDB Atlas using the provided connection link
(e.g., [mongodb+srv://S22Thesis1:123321@cluster0.k7tmgae.mongodb.net/]), which is
generated during the initial creation of the cluster, we gain access to various database
manipulation capabilities. These include batch updates and deletions of data within col-
lections.
Furthermore, MongoDB Compass facilitates data retrieval through query statements, al-
lowing us to specify criteria for retrieving desired data sets. Additionally, we can optimise
query performance by setting indexes within documents, thereby enhancing query effi-
ciency.
App Services
App Services is a powerful tool within MongoDB Atlas, designed to facil-
itate device synchronisation and data access. To initiate device synchronisation, the first
step involves creating an App user with full access privileges for the application. Subse-
quently, selecting an appropriate authentication approach for future connections becomes
imperative, which would decides the way you connect with the app, here we choose the
username and password. And the connection url would be: ‘Credentials.EmailPassword(”username”,
”password”)‘.
Upon successful connection to the app, the next crucial step is to establish rules for data
access. This entails assigning specific roles to each document based on the requirements
52
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:62==
Figure 4.18: The role for Patient Profile in App Service
for reading and writing data. By implementing these access rules, we ensure that the Unity
platform can securely retrieve and manipulate data from the database, fostering seamless
integration and functionality. The role for the ”Patient Profile” would show in fig4.18.
By leveraging MongoDB Atlas, MongoDB Compass, and App services, we successfully
implemented the database design, ensuring efficient deployment and access to the data.

4.2.5
Front-end Implementation
As mentioned in Section 2.6.2 the general setup of the front-end is implemented with Re-
act and Next.js. This section will provide an overview of the most important features used
from these technologies and frameworks, such as React hooks for state management,
Next.js routing for navigation, and integration with third-party libraries. The primary ob-
jective is to enable tasks outlined in Section 4.1.3 and to implement the general design
described in Section 4.1.5.2.
The folder structure within the project (Figure 4.19) is organised into distinct sections
for each of the stakeholders: patients, therapist and third parties. The ’layout.js’ file in
each section defines the general view for the specific page, serving as the root file. Un-
derneath it, there are subfiles that contain more specific code, determining the detailed
layout and functionalities of the elements on the page. This structure uses Next.js route
groups, which helps with organising the codebase into modular sections, simplifying nav-
igation and maintainability. Using Next.js route groups allows for a clear separation of
individual aspect of the project without affecting the URL paths, as the naming of the
route groups is simply for organisational purposes. It ensures that routes do not resolve
to the same URL path, avoiding conflicts. For example, ’(Marketing)/about/page.js’ and
’(Shop)/about/page.js’ would both resolve to ’/about’ and cause errors. Additionally, the
lack of a top-level ’layout.js’, the ’page.js’ file should be defined inside one of the route
groups. It is important to be aware that navigating across multiple root layouts will result in
a full page load instead of a client-side navigation. Therefore, implementing this routing
structure needs to be planned carefully, as it can significantly impact performance and
user experience. [108]
In React, both ’useState’ and ’useEffect’ are fundamental hooks that simplifies state man-
agement and side-effect handling within functional components, all of those hooks only
could be used at the top level of a React function (component), before any early returns.
To prevent the ’too many re-renders issue’, it is not allowed to use state as a rendering
dependency and change it at the same time, which would cause a loop in redering.
The ’useState’ hook allows you to add state variables to the components, enabling dy-
namic behaviour. State variables (Listing 4.1) hold information that can change over the
lifecycle of a component, such as user input, toggles, counters, or fetched data. When a
state variable is updated using the setter function provided by ’useState’, React re-renders

Listing 4.1: Example of useState and useEffect in React. And the usage of MUI library
and Next.js router.
The ’useEffect’ is used to perform side effects in the components, such as data fetch-
ing, subscriptions, or manually changing the DOM. Side-effects are operations that affect
something outside the scope of the current function, often incorporating with the asyn-
chronised functions. They include actions like making network requests to retrieve or
send data, setting up event listeners, updating the DOM, managing timers and logging
information. The ’useEffect’ hook runs after the first render and can be configured to run
again only when certain state variables or props change, controlled through the depen-
dency array provided as the second argument. If the dependency array is empty, the
effect runs only once after the initial render. If it does contain a variable, the effect runs
after every render where any of the variables has changed.
In Listing 4.1 ’useState’ is used to manage the ’patientsList’ and ’isAscending’ state vari-
ables, which can change based on user interaction. The ’useEffect’ hook is used to update
the value of the ’patientsList’. This setup ensures that the component displays the cor-
rect order of the ’patientsList’ once changing the ’isAscending’, and any updates to the
state ’isAscending’ would trigger a re-rendering to reflect the changes. By using ’useS-
tate’ and ’useEffect’, the front-end implementation ensures a efficient state management
and side-effect handling.
To enhance the UI, we utilise the MUI library as described in section 2.6.2. In the ex-
ample in listing 4.1 we use components like ’TextField’ to create searchable input field
and ’Button’ to toggle the sorting order. The integration of MUI components helps build a
consistent and visually appealing interface with minimal effort.
Additionally, we use the Next.js router for navigation within the application. The ’useRouter’
hook from Next.js allows us to specify which page we wish to switch to. In the example
in Listing 4.1 the ’handleButtonClick’ function demonstrates how we can navigate to dif-
ferent paths and pass data using the router. When a button is clicked, the function stores
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
55
==PAGE:65==
the necessary data in ’localStorage’ and then uses ’router.push’ to navigate to the spec-
ified path. The ’next/navigation’ module, which we use for routing is different from the
traditional way of handling routing ’next/router’. But with Next.js adding App Router, ’nex-
t/navigation’ was designed to offer a more streamlined and modern approach to routing.
It provides more features, including a stack for storing paths, which helps manage naviga-
tion history more effectively. The stack allows for a smoother transition and better control
over navigation actions, making it more useful in complex applications where navigational
history is important. [109]
The component structure in our project (Figure 4.19) is designed to ensure modularity,
reusability and maintainability, as highlighted in Section 2.6.2. Each component is focused
on a specific functionality or a set of related functionalities, which helps in keeping the
codebase clean and organised.
Components such as ’ClickBack’ as seen in Listing 4.2 is a utility component that pro-
vides a button for navigating back to the previous page. This component uses Next.js
’useRouter’ for navigation and MUIs components for styling.

This structure demonstrates that when elements and functions need to be used more
than once, it makes sense to create a component. This approach simplifies reusability
and consistency across different parts of the application, making it easier to maintain
and extend the codebase. More advanced setup like the WebGL library Three.js (Section
2.6.2), which involves much more code compared to a simple button, also benefit from this
approach. Reusing larger components saves time, and if specific changes are required
for a particular page, code can be passed into the component to customise it as needed
for that page. This means that the component is just a base template that can be further
adjusted if required.
4.2.5.1
Authentication
In our application, we implemented authentication to ensure that only authorised users
could access certain parts of the system. This is crucial for protecting sensitive data and
providing a personalised user experience. We utilise cookies for managing user sessions
and protecting routes using middleware.

Listing 4.3: How our CookieSetting is implemented in our code.
The listing 4.3 shows how we use ’useCookies’ hook from the ’react-cookie’ library to sim-
plify cookie management. The ’cookieSettings.js’ file exports a ’CookieSetting’ function,
which provides three primary funcationalities: ’getToken’, ’setToken’, and ’removeToken’.
These functions help manage the users session token stored in the cookies. The ’getTo-
ken’ function retrieves the current value of the ’user_token’ cookie with a specified value
and options such as expiration time or path. Once the user has signed in successfully, the
back-end would send a HTTP status code 200 with message ”success”, we would store
a token in the web application. The ’removeToken’ function removes the ’user_token’
cookie from the browser, which means that the user has logged out and would navigate
to log in page.
Securing Routes with Middleware
To ensure that only authenticated users can access
certain routes in our application, we implement route protection using middleware. This
approach allows us to intercept and validate requests before they reach the route handler,
meaning that only authenticated users can access certain parts. Our ’middleware.js’ file
contains the logic for managing user authentication status and handling sessions expira-
tion. We use the ’CookieSetting’ hook to access and manage the user token stored in
cookies. The middleware component checks for a valid session token and then redirects
users to the right pages based on their roles. To maintain the login status, we also set
to reset the token expiration time to 60 minutes whenever there is mouse movement or
keyboard activity within each 59-minutes interval.
1

import { reqLoginOUt } from "../api/api";

In the Listing 4.4 the ’useEffect’ hook periodically checks if the user’s session token is still
valid. If the token is not found or has expired, it triggers the ’handleUserExpire’ function,
which logs out the user and redirects them to the login page. Another ’useEffect’ hook
listens for changes in the user’s session token and redirects authenticated users to the
appropriate pages based on their role. If the user is not authenticated, they are redirected
to the login page. By using middleware to protect our routes, we can effectively man-
age user authentication and ensure that only authenticated users have access to specific
areas of the dashboard.

As seen in Listing 4.5 the scene and camera setup involves creating a perspective cam-
era positioned to provide a clear view of the avatar. The scene acts as a container for all

to the model, this however was not used as we did not have time to setup animations.

environment it can help in providing a more immersive experience.

Back-end development involves various technologies, like what we listed in Section 2,

4.2.6.1
API Design
The API design for our dashboard facilitates communication between the front-end and
back-end, utilising four HTTP methods: GET, POST, PUT, and DELETE. Currently, we
have implemented the GET and POST methods for data interaction.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
59
==PAGE:69==
Figure 4.20: MVC model
We chose not to strictly adhere to the standard RESTful API principles to prioritise read-
ability and maintainability. During the early stages of API design, many functional de-
tails were still under development, so naming the API based on its functionality provided
greater flexibility for future adjustments, and we also would choose to use same url path
with different request data for different operations, like the edit task basic information and
update task performance, we would not only send it with the patient’s id, but also with the
task JSON objects including all the updated information.
Below, we would describe the design of our APIs in details regarding to some specific
features, we mainly implemented the GET, POST in HTTP requesting Methods. And
Our endpoints use JSON objects or non-reference data types for message exchanges
because these formats are easier to serialise on the server side. Additionally, we handle
response errors using HTTP status codes with the JSON:
• 200: Response successfully.
• 400: Bad request.
• 500: Server error.
• 404: Page not found.

Spring Boot Implementation
This phrase describes the implementation of the back-end for our product using Spring

provides a support on MVC model, which helps us which helps in separating concerns
within the application. In the MVC model, the abbreviation of MVC represents three differ-

we separate the views from our back-end, mainly use the models and controllers to pro-

model used in our product would show in 4.20.

Data Mapping
: This paragraph introduces the entities within the models layer, which
represent the primary data structures utilised for processing microservices. Each entity
maps to a collection in the database and is serialised using Java IO operations for subse-
quent use within the Java project. Since Spring Data MongoDB provides an abstraction
over the MongoDB Java driver, we could use the annotations directly in the class for data
persistence , such as using ‘@Document‘ to specify the collection, ‘@Id‘ and ‘@Field‘ to
map the attributes, it greatly reduced the amount of our code. The following outlines the
definition and relationships of each entity:
• Patient: Represents a patient profile added into the database via the feedback sys-
tem.
• Therapist: Represents a therapist profile within the application(imported manually
through a JSON file).
• Caregiver: Represents a caregiver profile within the application(imported manually
through a JSON file).
• Game: An embedded entity within the Task model, indicating the game information
associated with a task.
• Task: Represents a exercise task assigned by the therapist to a specific patient.
62
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:72==
• TaskStatus: An enumeration model used to verify the status of a task, facilitating
task statistics for the patient.
• Performance: An embedded entity within the Task model, stored as an array to
represent the performance metrics of each task set.
• Contact: An embedded entity within the Patient model, containing information about
the patient’s contact person, corresponding to a specific caregiver.
These entities constructs a well structured model layer, which is clear and easy to use in
the business services, optimising the efficient data processing within the microservices
architecture.

Service Layer and RESTful API Controllers
: In general, the service layer should
be separated from the controller layer to maintain a clear separation of concerns. How-
ever, for our feedback system, the business requirements are relatively small, allowing
us to combine the service layer with the controller layer. This approach enables us to
handle HTTP requests and process the necessary business logic sequentially, returning
responses based on the processing results.
The implementation of RESTful API Controllers also leverages the Spring annotations to
define endpoints and manage request parameters, validation, and error handling. The
functionality of those annotations would be listed in the below:
• ‘@CrossOrigin‘: Enabling the cross origin requests.
• ‘@RestController‘: Marking a class as the controller to interact with the front-end
via RESTful API.
• ‘@RequestMapping(”/api”)‘: Categorising the requests for each controller by giv-
ing it a united name.
• ‘@PutMapping‘ and ‘@GetMapping‘: Map to the HTTP requests based on the
request type set in the front-end.
• ‘@RequestBody‘: Combining it with the ResponseEntity to return a serialised JSON
result.
The business logic processing and repository function calls occur between receiving the
request and returning the ResponseEntity. The following example demonstrates user
authentication in the combined service and controller layer, where we could see the com-
bination of those two layer clearly:
1
package com.example.hdt.controller;

Redis And Spring Cache
: In our system, Redis is primarily used for caching data,
facilitated by Spring Cache. The integration of Redis serves two main purposes. Firstly,
it serves as a variable that will not be destroyed when the program is closed and is used
to verify the status of operations during the process of some business logic. Secondly, it
stores the list of active patients in the cache. If there are no changes within the active pa-
tients’ profiles, the list is retrieved directly from memory, which prevents frequent requests
sending without any operations in the client side. Otherwise, the old cache is discarded
and a new list is cached from database. The data retrieval structure is illustrated in Figure
4.22.
