: To deploy our SpringBoot and Next.js projects using Docker,
follow these general steps:
• Create a Dockerfile: This file contains instructions for building a Docker image,
specifying the project’s environment and dependencies. The front-end and back-
end are separated.
• Build and Run the Docker Image: Using terminal to build the image by ‘docker
build <IMAGE-NAME>:<TAG> .‘, and run the image by ‘docker run –name <NAME-
TO-BE-SHOW> -p8080:8080 -d <IMAGE_ID>‘.
• Compose-up: To compose the front-end and back-end images into one container,
using ‘docker-compose up‘.
• Run the Docker Container: Deploy the application once the containers are up and
running by running a container from the built image in Docker Desktop.
4.3
Game Platform
In this section, we introduce our game platform, covering its setup, UI design, gameplay
design, HDT embodiment, and crucial technical implementations. This discussion will be
followed by an overview of the platform architecture and a summary. Our aim is to highlight
the integration of HDT and the VR-Sensor gameplay in upper-limb exercise, which forms
the core focus of our research.
4.3.1
Device Setup
Our game platform setup comprises three main components: the VR platform where the
game takes place, the motion sensors serving as the controlling input for the games, and
the exercise equipment on which the sensors are mounted, enabling users to engage in
physical activity. Specifically, the following devices were utilised:
• PICO NEO 3 Pro VR headsets, featuring Bluetooth 5.1 connectivity, Android 10
operating system, and hand controllers for interaction. The headset should connect
to a network so that it can load the user data from database.
66
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:76==
• Syncsense motion sensors, incorporating an Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU) with
accelerometer and gyroscope capabilities, enabling wireless communication via Blue-
tooth Low Energy (BLE). These sensors are equipped with adhesive backing, allow-
ing them to be mounted on stickers or bands.
• Exercise equipment including hand cyclers, dumbbells of varying weights, and a
manual wheelchair outfitted with rollers underneath.
Figure 4.23: Sensor setup for the hand cycler, dumbbell, and manual wheelchair. Sensors
are marked with red rectangles.
The mounting of the motion sensors on the exercise equipment was a crucial consid-
eration, ensuring that the sensors could sensitively detect movement without impeding
normal exercise actions or being placed in areas where users might touch them. Based
on these criteria, the sensors were positioned as follows: for hand cyclers, a single sensor
was affixed to the side of one pedal, as the left and right pedals are interconnected. for
dumbbells, the sensors were bundled at the edges; and for the wheelchair, sensors were
mounted on both the left and right rollers underneath the chair, enabling independent
tracking of left and right inputs.
4.3.2
Game Platform
In this section, we present the UI design and the fundamental features of our game plat-
form. The interaction mechanism is analogous to the familiar process of using a mouse to
click a button on a laptop. In our VR environment, users utilize either the left or right hand
controller to point at and select a button displayed within the VR headset. The controllers
are responsive and precise, allowing for accurate selection and interaction.
The interface shown in this section represents our final implementation of the design,
which differs slightly from the one described in Section 4.1.4.1. These differences are
due to time constraints and the inclusion of features for specific testing purposes. We
prioritise the development of features based on their importance.
4.3.2.1
Main Page
The main page of our game platform serves as the central hub for various functions and
activities. Here are the key features and their functions:
• Pair: Click on ”Pair” to connect the sensors with the headset when launching the
game for the first time.
• ROM: Click on ”ROM” to measure the shoulder abduction Range of Motion (ROM).
Users will be guided through an exercise to raise their arms as high as possible,
with the system displaying the maximum raising angle after a few seconds.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
67
==PAGE:77==
• Account: Click on ”Account” to login or switch between users.
• Play Games: Click on ”Play Games” to navigate to the Game Selection page, where
users can choose from various game options available on the platform.
Figure 4.24: Main page with a default avatar before choosing the user.
The Shoulder Abduction Range of Motion (ROM) page guides users through exercises
to measure their shoulder mobility. Users are instructed to raise their arms as high as
possible, while the avatar in the VR environment mirrors their movements in real-time.
After a few seconds, the system calculates and displays the maximal raising angle, pro-
viding immediate feedback on the user’s shoulder abduction ROM. This feature helps both
users and therapists monitor progress and track user performance, providing immediate
feedback on the user’s shoulder abduction ROM.
Figure 4.25: Shoulder abduction ROM. The avatar will mirror user movement.
The Account Page allows users to log in to their accounts. We considered two design
approaches for this feature. The first approach involves users logging in and out manually
by entering their login details. This method is suitable for scenarios where individual user
privacy and security are paramount, and each user is expected to manage their account
independently. However, it can be time-consuming and cumbersome, especially when
frequent user switches are required.
The second approach, which we ultimately chose, involves therapists selecting the current
68
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:78==
user by clicking on their name from a displayed list. This design is particularly beneficial
in our specific scenario, where a group of users and a therapist, who all know each other,
are physically in the same room while playing the game. Since the therapist manages
VR game exercises, this approach streamlines the process by eliminating the need for
frequent logins and logouts.
User names are fetched from a MongoDB database, ensuring synchronisation with the
dashboard. This integration guarantees that any changes or updates made to user ac-
counts are immediately reflected across the platform, maintaining consistency and relia-
bility. We chose this latter design because our product testing primarily occurs in environ-
ments where therapists and users interact closely in the same physical space. This setup
allows for a more efficient and seamless user experience, tailored to the specific needs
of our target scenario.

4.3.2.2
Sensor Pairing and Calibration
Sensor pairing is done only once when the game is first launched in the headset, typically
during the initial setup of the device and equipment. Clicking on the ”Pair” button on
the main page navigates to the Pairing page. This process is necessary because we
operate in a scenario where multiple VR headsets and multiple motion sensors are used
simultaneously in the same room. To ensure accurate data collection and synchronisation,
the headset must identify and connect with the corresponding sensors. This allows the
system to request motion data correctly when performing different exercises, ensuring
seamless operation and precise tracking.
70
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:80==
Figure 4.30: Pairing Menu Page
In the pairing process, the VR headset first connects to as many sensors as possible and
subscribes to their motion data. It then asks the user to move each corresponding sensor
one at a time while making other sensors idle. The user must keep moving the sensor
to fill a progress bar, avoiding random mistakes. After pairing, the sensor addresses are
stored locally in the VR headset, so the headset can automatically search for and connect
to the sensors needed when a game starts. Pairing is required for each exercise, such
as hand cycling, dumbbell raising, and manual wheelchair use.
Figure 4.31: Pairing Process for wheelchair
During the pairing process, as the headset receives motion data from the sensors, cal-
ibration for some games can also be performed. For example, in the wheelchair game,
sensors are mounted on two wheels separately. During the pairing process, the game
instructs users to move each wheel forward one at a time, allowing the system to recog-
nise the pattern of forward and backward movements. Since the sensors are mounted
on the rollers under the wheelchair, they rotate around an axis parallel to the rollers when
the user rolls the wheels. This calibration ensures the game can determine which kind of
movement, specifically rotating around which axis, corresponds to moving forward. The
opposite direction on the same axis indicates moving backward. This allows us to im-
plement a feature that accurately simulates the forward and backward movement in the
wheelchair game, closely mimicking how a real wheelchair operates.

4.3.3
Gameplay
4.3.3.1
Cycling
• Description: This is a bike racing game set in a mountainous scenario where the
player races against a ”ghost” bike. The ghost bike can either be a default oppo-
nent, represented by an empty bike with no avatar, or the player’s past performance,
represented by a bike with the user’s avatar. The track is generated randomly each
time, featuring variations in height and direction to enhance the challenge and in-
terest. The default opponent bike adjusts its speed based on the player’s position:
it decreases speed when ahead of the player and increases speed when falling be-
hind. We will describe our game according to the components mentioned in Section
2.3.
72
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:82==
Figure 4.32: Player races against a default opponent
Figure 4.33: Player races against past performance
• Implicit Objective: Improve upper-limb muscular endurance.
• Explicit Objective: Race against the opponent.
• Challenge: The opponent will mirror player’s past performance, so as the play mak-
ing progress, the opponent will become faster too.
• Interacting/Exercising Way: A sensor is mounted on the hand pedal. Players roll the
hand pedals to move forward, with the bike automatically steering itself. Players only
need to roll to apply force to the bike. Since the left and right pedals are connected,
left and right inputs cannot be separated.
• Duration: The game is designed to last around 15 minutes to provide sufficient aer-
obic exercise intensity while minimising the discomfort of wearing a VR headset.
• Input from Sensor: The game uses the relative motion intensity from the sensor as
input. ”Relative” means that the value is divided by a baseline to normalise it.

4.3.4
Human Digital Twin Embodiment
To study HDT embodiment in the game, we use various approaches and technologies
from three differnt aspects, visual embodiment, voice embodiment, performance embod-
iment.
4.3.4.1
Visual Embodiment: User Own Avatar
An avatar is created by the user on our web dashboard, which integrates the Ready Player
Me panel[58]. The URL(Uniform Resource Locator) of the avatar’s GLB (Graphics Library
Transmission Format Binary file) model is stored in the database. The game fetches the
user avatar from the database and loads it in the game. As previously mentioned, users
can see their avatar in several places: the main page, the greeting page just before the
game starts, and the avatar round panel in every game. This design fosters a strong
sense of attachment between the users and the game platform.
Considering that our test users predominantly use wheelchairs in their daily lives, we
designed a blue futuristic projector under the avatar to conceal the avatar’s legs. This
design choice enhances the sense of self-identity and relatability towards the avatar.
The avatar is equipped with both body and facial animations, facilitated by Inworld.AI[60].
The avatar stands still when not talking and performs animations while talking or when
first appearing on screen. This dynamic visual embodiment further immerses users in the
game, enhancing their overall experience.
4.3.4.2
Voice Embodiment: Voice Companion
During the game and on the greeting page before the game starts, the user avatar en-
gages in conversation with the user using predefined content designed to guide and moti-
vate. The avatar also speaks the user’s name to create a more personalized experience.
78
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:88==
Depending on the user’s gender, the game features both male and female voices, which
are warm and welcoming. Currently, due to technical constraints, the avatar can only
speak in English.
Below are some examples of the speaking content under specific conditions during the
cycling game. Similar content is used for the other games. The complete voice sentences
can be found in appendix L.
• When the Cycling game just starts: ”Let’s paddle! user name! Turn the hand cycle
round and round to move ahead. The other bike represents your previous perfor-
mance. Push harder and try to outdo your past! When you are ready, move to
start!”
• When the user plays for three minutes: ”You’ve been moving for three minutes! user
name! Keep it up!”
• When the user is behind the opponent: ”Feeling slow? user name! That’s okay!
Let’s speed up together!”
• When the user is ahead of the opponent: ”You’re fast! user name! I’m trying to keep
up with you!”
• When the user wins: ”You won! user name! You’re stronger than before and set a
new record! Move to continue.”
• When the user loses: ”You finished! user name! Great effort! Next time, you might
just beat me! Move to continue.”
Thus, the user will be playing the game along with a voice companion that provides in-
structions and real-time feedback, motivating the user to complete the game and achieve
their exercise goals.

sentences into YAML format, and use ”trigger” attribute to initiate the speech.

4.3.4.3
Performance Embodiment: Compete with Past Performance
In addition to visual and voice embodiment, the final aspect is performance embodiment.
This feature allows users to compete against their past performances, providing a tangible
way to measure progress and enhance motivation. When a user plays a racing game,
the game records their performance and stores it. The next time the user plays, the game
replays the previous performance as the opponent, enabling the user to race against
their past performance and strive to improve. Currently, this feature is only available in
the Cycling game, as hand cycling is the most common exercise among our test users
compared to manual wheelchair use and dumbbell raising.
We implemented this feature by concatenating the game input from the sensors into a
string every 0.5 seconds. After the game finishes, this string is stored in a local file named
userName.userInfo on the VR headset. The next time the user plays the game, the stored
string is read and applied to the opponent bike every 0.5 seconds.
Using the performance embodiment technique provides users with additional motivation
while playing the game, allowing them to see how well they perform compared to their
last session and making their progress visible.

4.3.5
Gameplay Implementation
4.3.5.1
Range of Motion Measurement with Motion Sensor
In this section, we discuss how to measure shoulder abduction ROM using a motion sen-
sor and its potential application for game control. By mounting a sensor on the wrist
with the XZ plane parallel to the arm, the shoulder abduction ROM can be determined by
measuring the rotational angle of the sensor around the X-axis. This method provides an
accurate and reliable means to quantify shoulder abduction, which can then be translated
into game controls. This integration not only enhances the gaming experience but also
can provide valuable feedback for fitness progress.
80
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:90==
(a) Shoulder Abduction[110]
(b) ROM of shoulder abduction is equal to
wrist-mounted sensor Z-axis rotation angle.
Figure 4.39: Shoulder Abduction Range of Motion(ROM) Measurement
In theory, we can measure this angle either by accelerometer or gyroscope.
The accelerometer measures the acceleration around each axis, including gravitational
acceleration. When the sensor is stationary, the acceleration values of each axis reflect
its deviation from the vertical direction, or equivalently, the direction of gravity.
θAcc = acos( Accz
gravity)
The gyroscope, on the other hand, measures rotational speed in terms of degrees per
second, allowing us to accumulate the total rotation angle around the X-axis.
θt
Gyro = θt−1
Gyro + Gyrox ∗∆t
However, both accelerometers and gyroscopes have inherent measurement errors. The
accelerometers angle is calculated directly based on Accz, which may include external
accelerations. Although it contains noise, it does not drift over time, making it relatively
stable in the long term. The gyroscope’s angle measurement is less noisy and provides
precise results over short durations, but small errors can accumulate over time, leading
to drift. Thus, while it is accurate in the short term, its long-term reliability diminishes.
To overcome these limitations, Complementary Filter(CF) combines the advantage of
each sensor. It calculates the angle using a combination of accelerometer and gyroscope
data, resulting in a more accurate measurement. The CF effectively balances the short-
term accuracy of the gyroscope with the long-term stability of the accelerometer.[111]
θ = α ∗θAcc + (1 −α) ∗θGyro
The CF’s implementation in C# demonstrates its computational efficiency and ease of use,
making it a practical choice for real-time applications in measuring shoulder abduction
ROM.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
81
==PAGE:91==
1
public float ComplementaryFilterRotation(float gx, float az, float angle,
float weightAcc)
2
{
3
// Clamp ax value within range
4
if (az < -Mathf.Abs(gravity)) az = -Mathf.Abs(gravity);
5
else if (az > Mathf.Abs(gravity)) az = Mathf.Abs(gravity);
6
7
// Calculate thetaAcc (angle from accelerometer)
8
float thetaAcc = Mathf.Acos(az / gravity) * Mathf.Rad2Deg;
9
10
// Calculate thetaRot (angle from gyroscope)
11
float sign = az >= 0 ? 1 : -1;
12
float thetaRot = angle + gx * sign * Time.deltaTime;
13
14
// Calculate the final angle using the complementary filter
15
return weightAcc * thetaAcc + (1 - weightAcc) * thetaRot;
16
}

4.3.5.2
Track Generation
Tracks are widely used in our game platform, including in the cycling, wheelchair racing,
and wheelchair football games. We developed a random track generation system, which
allows for the configuration of track segment prefabs, track length, horizontal variation,
vertical variation, and finish line customisation.
In procedurally generated game scenes, such as natural landscapes, a noise algorithm is
essential for placing game objects in a natural position automatically. For track generation,
we prepare a track segment prefab and use a noise algorithm to place it in the game.
Perlin noise is a widely-used noise algorithm and is integrated into the Unity engine.
The Perlin Noise algorithm uses an arbitrary grid with points on it and interpolates the
pixels between those points. Each point has a gradient vector that influences the inter-
polated pixels. The value at each pixel is determined by the dot product computed by
the gradient vector[112]. We apply the Perlin noise algorithm in Unity to generate tracks
with natural horizontal and vertical variations, enhancing the realism and complexity of
the game environment.
1
public IEnumerator GenerateTrack()
2
{
3
for (int i = 0; i < numberOfSegments; i++)
4
{
5
float perlinX = Mathf.PerlinNoise(noiseOffsetX + i *
perlinNoiseScale , 0) - 0.5f;
6
float perlinY = Mathf.PerlinNoise(0, noiseOffsetY + i *
perlinNoiseScale) - 0.5f;
7
float perlinZ = currentDirection.z;
8
9
Vector3 directionChange = new Vector3(perlinX *
horizontalVariation , perlinY * verticalVariation , perlinZ) *
directionChangeIntensity;
10
11
Vector3 nextPosition = currentPosition + lastDirection *
segmentLength;
12
GameObject segment = Instantiate(trackSegmentPrefab , nextPosition ,
Quaternion.LookRotation(currentDirection));
13
currentPosition = nextPosition;
14
lastDirection = currentDirection;

Listing 4.18: Track generation using Perlin Noise algorithm
Besides the noise algorithm utilised here, we have implemented two programming ap-
proaches for track generation. The first approach generates the complete track before the
game starts, which works well for small-scale games but does not scale efficiently. The
second approach generates a track of a certain length and procedurally moves track seg-
ments from the back to the front as the game progresses. This method is more memory-
efficient and scales well for longer racing games. It only needs to remember the position
of the last-generated track segment and use the Perlin Noise algorithm again to place the
new track segment in front of it.
We follow the Object Pool programming pattern, which involves moving track segments
around instead of constantly instantiating and destroying them. This approach maintains
good performance by reusing game objects, reducing the overhead associated with fre-
quent object creation and destruction.
1
public IEnumerator RegenerateTracks()
2
{
3
GameObject segment = tracksQueue.Peek();
4
PerlinNoiseGenerate(segment);
5
tracksQueue.Dequeue();
6
tracksQueue.Enqueue(segment);
7
}
Listing 4.19: Procedurally track generation using Perlin Noise algorithm and Object Pool
programming pattern.
This track generation algorithm is used in three games and effectively supports the game-
play. Additionally, it is worth mentioning that the bike or wheelchair can use the forward
direction of the current track segment to determine the correct driving direction and turn
accordingly. This ensures smooth and realistic movement along the procedurally gener-
ated tracks.
4.3.5.3
Jumping Mechanism in JumpJump
In the JumpJump game, each tower is generated at runtime with random heights and
directions. The player needs to jump to the next tower using accumulated motion intensity
as input, avoiding falls that could create a frustrating experience or induce panic in players
with acrophobia. To achieve this, the player’s position is explicitly set in the script, moving
them from the current tower to the next.
To ensure that the jumps appear natural, we move the player along a Bezier Curve.
A Bezier Curve is a smooth and continuous curve, which helps in creating natural and
smooth jump movements. It is defined by a set of control points and a few parameters,
making it computationally efficient to use during runtime. For this game, we use a Cubic
Bezier Curve with four control points: the first point is the player’s current position, and
the last point is the top of the next tower. The two intermediate control points are adjusted
to shape the trajectory of the jump.
Using the Bezier Curve method ensures that the player’s movement between towers feels
fluid and realistic, enhancing the overall gaming experience. This approach makes the
jumps visually appealing and consistent.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout

4.3.6
Summary
Figure 4.40: Game Platform Architecture. In the main page, user first pairs the sensors
and its info will be stored in local files. After choosing the current user in the account, the
avatar controller will be able to load the avatar and username from the database. The
game consists of three main parts: HDT, gameplay of four games, and SensorManager
with four interacting ways corresponding to the four games. HDT is integrated into the
gameplay to create a personalised immersive experience.
84
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:94==
The main page of our virtual reality fitness game platform serves as the initial interface
where users can pair sensors, select their profiles, and choose games to play. Sensor
pairing information is stored in local files, which the system uses to establish correct con-
nections during gameplay. Upon selecting a user on the main page, the platform loads
the user’s avatar and username from a database in real-time.
The gameplay part of the platform is divided into three main components: HDT, gameplay
mechanics, and SensorManager. The HDT component includes a visual avatar created by
the user, a voice companion that aligns with the user’s gender, and a feature for recording
and replaying performance data to mirror the user’s actions. The gameplay mechanics
involve four distinct games: Cycling, JumpJump, Wheelchair Racing, and Wheelchair
Football. SensorManager supports four different interaction methods based on the type
of exercise and corresponding game, such as relative motion intensity for Cycling, relative
rotation intensity for Wheelchair Racing, accumulated motion intensity for JumpJump, and
forward and backward rotation for Wheelchair Football.
We have developed four games that utilise motion data as input, each featuring a unique
interaction method and associated exercise equipment. Our design philosophy aims to
integrate the interaction more intrinsically with the equipment. For instance, players cycle
using a hand cycler, control wheelchairs using a physical wheelchair, and simulate jump-
ing by raising dumbbells. By embedding our VR game into users’ daily fitness exercises
and mounting sensors directly onto the equipment, we eliminate the need for external
devices beyond a VR headset. This approach underscores the potential of using simple
motion sensors as game inputs, offering an intuitive and engaging gaming experience.
Additionally, we have incorporated HDT into the platform to enhance gameplay. This
involves a personalised avatar and voice interactions, providing a more immersive and
supportive gaming environment. The cycling game, in particular, explores the concept
of competing against past performance, showcasing the advanced capabilities of HDT in
gaming.
Our development process is guided by the Mechanics, Dynamics, and Aesthetics (MDA)
framework [83]. We integrate HDT embodiment—including personalised avatars, voice
guides and encouragement, and performance recording and replay—into the Mechanics
component, along with other game elements such as sound, racing gameplay, maps, and
speedometers. This integration results in a more intuitive and personalised Dynamics
system. Consequently, in the Aesthetics component, users experience a more engaging
and motivating game environment.
In conclusion, our VR game platform, enhanced by HDT embodiment and intrinsic motion-
based controls, offers a more effective and enjoyable experience for upper-limb exercise.
By integrating these advanced features, we aim to transform fitness exercises into an
engaging and motivating activity, ultimately contributing to improved the outcomes.
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout

control through small motion sensors. The MDA framework is used to dissect the game’s
design, focusing on how HDT enhances personalised gameplay and how motion sensors
contribute to more natural and intuitive player interactions.

5
Evaluation
The evaluation chapter details the testing procedure. It will present the interview process,
hypotheses to be evaluated, highlighting feedback gathered from students, therapists,
and third-party users. The results section will present findings for each group. Presenting
the students engagement and satisfaction with the VR experiences, the therapists insights
on the effectiveness and usability of the dashboard, and the third-party users perspective
on their dashboard functionality. And finally the hypothesis will be discussed as to how
the found results indicates support or not.
5.1
Testing
The actual testing took place at a specialised education and activity center, designed
for individuals with various disabilities and special needs. The center offer educational
programs and activities, primarily work with young people with conditions such as autism,
ADD, ADHD, cerebral palsy, communication disabilities, developmental disabilities, down
syndrome, and other rare disabilities [113].
We presented the general idea and showcased our early prototypes to the administra-
tion and some of the therapists. They seemed enthusiastic about the project and were
willing to proceed with testing alongside some of their students. From this, we were as-
signed nine participants who were deemed suitable and were themselves willing to assist
us in testing our solution. Flyers (Appendix I) with information and consent forms were
provided. Their parents or guardians also received information about the project and con-
sent forms were also provided, as we were also interested in having their feedback on the
third party dashboard (Section 4.1.1).
Our testing room was set up next to their fitness rehabilitation room (Figure 5.1), pro-
viding space for a wheelchair and two arm cycles. We also brought four VR headsets,
two of which had our game (Section 4.3) installed, while the other two were provided
by Syncsense where their proprietary software was installed1. All VR experiences were
connected to sensors (Section 4.3) provided by Syncsense.
The primary objective of the interviews conducted with the students and therapists was
therefore, to evaluate the impact of integrating HDT elements in serious-game based
fitness. Based on the Technology Acceptance Model in Section 3.4.1, which focuses on
perceived usefulness and ease of use in technology adoption, we specifically formulate
four hypotheses to evaluate the HDT embodiment into serious game based fitness. By
leveraging TAM, we aim to understand how HDT could impact engagement, effectiveness,
and overall acceptance of our system. The following hypotheses were developed to guide
our evaluation through interviews with students and therapists.
• H1: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness contribute to the perceived use-
fulness of the system through increased user engagement and perceived personal-
isation.
• H2: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are associated with the system’s
perceived ease of use.
• H3: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are linked to system likeability
and user enjoyment.
1Pre-recorded VR films that can be experienced by the user through VR glasses (Appendix K)[114]
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
87
==PAGE:97==
Figure 5.1: Students and therapists in the fitness room where we conducted our tests
• H4: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are related to users’ willingness
to incorporate the system into their everyday fitness routines.
5.1.1
Procedure
Overall the testing was conducted over five days in three weeks. We had nine students
and six therapists participating in the test. A timetable was provided by the therapists,
while most students participated in four to five testing sessions, one student only attended
the last two sessions because of illness. Each testing day consisted of five hours, some-
times each student had their own therapist, while sometimes one therapist was respon-
sible for a pair of students. Of the nine students, six uses wheelchairs daily, of these six
students, one can occasionally stand and sit on other chairs, while the others remained
seated. Three students had one hand with less functionality than the other, and one ex-
hibited hand tremors, affecting their ability to hold objects and apply force. For students
with difficulty using one hand, therapists often used a band to fix their arms on the hand
cycle. During the sessions, the therapists helped motivate their students, engaging in
typical conversation, and translating between danish and english, and making sure the
resistance on the hand cycles were appropriate for each student.
During their first session, the students was actively included in creating their own avatar,
deciding how they wanted their digital twin to look and dress. Afterwards, the students
tried both VR experience, our games and the Syncsense films (Appendix K). Typically
one student started with our cycling game for 15 minutes, and if there were two students
present, the other would play a Syncsense film of their choice, also lasting 15 minutes.
Afterwards a switch was made between the two, and this would repeat until 45 minutes
were used, which meant an average of two or three VR games per student. After playing
each of the games, they rated their experience using a scale from a sad emoji to a happy
emoji (Appendix H) , indicating their satisfaction. On the final day of testing, a survey was
conducted with eight students and six therapists. Following the survey, therapists and
students participated in interviews separately.
From the groups perspective, each day of testing, we took notes on observations, includ-
ing those related to the students, therapists and any technical issues or processes that
needed adjustment. This meant compiling a logbog (Appendix J) throughout each day
88
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:98==
Figure 5.2: Students and therapists in the fitness room where we conducted our tests
of testing. At the end of day, we each took a few minutes to reflect on our notes and
thoughts before discussing our observations and deciding on any necessary changes.
Issues ranged from alternative solutions to our platform malfunctions. For instance, user
avatars were not saved automatically into the database, requiring us to manually copy and
paste avatar model data directly into the database. Another issue was the scheduling of
students time slots, which were too closely set. This did not account for the time needed
for students to remove the headset, evaluate the game, and for those in wheelchairs,
navigate out of the confined space before new students were coming with their own
wheelchairs. The limited transition time also caused us to overlook procedures such as
disinfecting the headsets and charging them. Consequently, testing sessions had to be
shortened from one hour to 45 minutes.
5.1.1.1
Technical Issues
During field testing, we encountered some issues. First, an issue related to concurrency,
specifically caused by the read-write lock in MongoDB. When the Unity game was han-
dling data in the database, we couldn’t fetch the corresponded data in the web dash-
board, leading to significant delays and inconsistencies. To address this problem, we
restructured our approach to database communication. We transitioned the Unity game
to interact with the database through a set of RESTful APIs instead of direct database ac-
cess. This change decouples the game from the database, allowing for more controlled
and efficient data handling.
To solve the first issue, we revised our structure, the Unity game now sends requests to a
back-end that exposes RESTful endpoints. This back-end server manages all database
interactions, ensuring that read and write operations are handled more efficiently and
reducing the risk of concurrency-related issues. By implementing this API layer, we im-
proved data consistency and system reliability. The updated structure is shown below:
This new structure not only resolves the concurrency issues but also provides a more
scalable and maintainable architecture for future enhancements. However, since we im-
plemented this updated structure after the field testing phase, we have not yet had the
opportunity to test it in a real-world scenario. Despite this, we are confident that the new
setup will improve system functionality and reliability based on our comprehensive testing
and analysis.
Secondly, for the 3D avatars we used in Unity game, they were all uploaded in the database
manually. The user avatar is created in Ready Player Me and import in Inworld.ai, since
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
89
==PAGE:99==
Figure 5.3: The updated communication structure between applications with database
Inworld.ai integrate the avatar with animation and voice in Unity, as mentioned in Section
2.6.5. This was a temporary solution for testing, with potential future enhancements to
automate the process. Apart from the technical issue in dashboard, we also encountered
issues in the games. In the Cycling game, we initially enabled users to compete against a
normal cycle, which had no avatar, and with the default speed, to familiarise themselves
with the game. In the game session before the avatar competition session, we asked
them to ride as fast as possible trying achieve their best performance. In the final ses-
sion, they competed against their own previous performance and with their avatar as an
opponent on the other bike.
Then, for the Wheelchair Racing game, it was tested by two students on the first day,
but we stopped testing it in subsequent testing for three reasons. First, most students
were using wheelchairs and could not easily transfer themselves to the wheelchair for
playing the game. Second, the manual wheelchair setup in the testing spot couldn’t be
fixed properly above the roller, which kept moving to a side and made it difficult to roll the
wheelchair. Third, there is a technical problem with the loading of the avatar in these two
games, where the user avatars couldn’t be displayed in them.
Finally, regarding to the Wheelchair Football, since our testing period for each student is
limited and this game might cause cybersickness to students, let alone the three issues for
Wheelchair Racing also apply for Wheelchair Football. We put it on least priority among
the four games and didn’t have time for testing it.
Overall, we encountered five issues while we were testing our system at Maglemosen.
As a result were able to effectively solve three out of the five issues, either with temporary
fixes or more permanent solutions.
5.1.1.2
Iteration during the testing
JumpJump
In the initial design of the JumpJump game, players were instructed to raise
a specific arm in a particular abduction manner. However, during the testing phase, it
became evident that this approach felt unnatural for most users. Players expressed a
90
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:100==
preference for performing actions at their own pace and in a more freeform manner. Ad-
ditionally, some participants struggled with understanding the English prompts provided.
To address these issues, we revised the interaction mechanism to utilise accumulated
motion intensity as the primary game input. This approach mirrors the input method used
in the cycling game. In the revised system, the game records the current motion intensity
and sums it until a predefined threshold is reached, at which point the player character
jumps to the next tower. This change allows players to use either both arms or a single
arm, thereby enhancing accessibility and accommodating a wider range of player prefer-
ences.
The sensor input was modified to capture accumulated motion intensity from both sensors
during the iterative testing process.

eliminating language barriers and providing clearer guidance.

Others
We made changes in the UI Design during the testing phase. On the first testing
day, we guided the students to create their own avatars. The students expressed a desire
to see every part of the avatars they created, including the pants and shoes. But before in
the dashboards, we designed a hologram effect (Section 4.1.5.2) to hide the legs. We de-
cided to remove that effect in the dashboard and therapists and the third-party caregivers
according to the feedback. We did not remove it in the game platform, because students
were not able to check that page. We always put headsets on them after choosing a game
because they did not use controllers.
5.2
Survey
The survey for therapists is designed based on the task scenarios to give a better un-
derstanding of the entire product. Firstly, we demonstrated task scenarios where a new
student needed to be added to the system. Task one was to create a profile for the student.
Then, we assumed the student had completed their first therapy session and learned how
to use the system and devices so they would exercise independently without the thera-
pist’s accompaniment. Task two was adding several exercise tasks with games for the
new student. After completing the tasks, we presented therapists with the client details
page of students in the therapists dashboard, including basic information, task achieve-
ments, task editing, and data visualisation of ROM. Lastly, they filled out a survey with
questions about the task scenarios, client details page and the overview effectiveness
and usability of the product. The content of task scenarios and the survey can be found
in Appendix E
We also designed a survey for third-party caregivers with the intention of collecting their
feedback on using the dashboard and their perspective on the avatar. After the user
testing session, we sent guardians of students emails with the online survey. The survey
can be found in the Appendix F. However, we did not receive any response or feedback
from the third-party caregivers.
5.3
Interview
On the final day of testing, interviews were conducted with both students and therapists.
We gathered feedback from a total of eight students and six therapists, all of whom had
participated throughout the entire of testing period, with the exception of one of the thera-
pists who were present for two out of five days. In alignment with the evaluation methods
outlined in Section 3.4 and in collaboration with our co-supervisor, Martin Wolfgang Lauer-
Schmaltz, we created questionnaires aimed at addressing our research hypotheses which
would help us towards our primary research question.
The interview questions are designed according to the hypotheses. Questions for stu-
dents focus on their understanding of digital twins, enjoyment and motivation in using the
system compared to normal exercises, preferences between competing against a default
bike or their digital twin, clarity and identification with their avatar, and their interest in
integrating the system into their daily rehabilitation process. The questions for therapists
address understanding and perception of digital twins, how useful the system is compared
to traditional rehabilitation, motivational elements, benefits, intuitiveness of the therapist
dashboard, satisfaction with the system, and interest in integrating similar systems.
5.3.1
Data Collection Constraints
Some of the important limitations should be kept in mind when reading the actual results
from the questionnaires.
92
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:102==
Students
The students only spoke Danish, requiring the questions to be asked in En-
glish and then immediately translated into Danish by the therapists. Some students also
had difficulties with communication and self-expression. The therapists facilitated this
process by translating quantitative questions into qualitative phrases, indicating agree-
ment or disagreement. Additionally, the testing conditions were not as controlled as we
would have wanted, while one student was answering questions, another was present in
the room playing a VR game.
Therapists
Due to technical issues, therapists could not be provided with an online
experience of the dashboard as we have initially planned. Consequently, their feedback
is based on limited usage with the dashboard. Moreover, the usability questions were in
English, which again required them to translate from English to Danish. Similar to the
students, other therapists were also present in the room when the questionnaires was
carried out.
Third Party
For the third party users, including parents and guardians, technical issues
prevented us from providing an online working version where they could follow their child’s
or wards progress in real-time. Therefore, their feedback is based solely on viewing the
the statistics of completed progress, without the ability to live stream the in game view.
5.4
Results
This section presents the feedback gathered from the survey, interviews, and daily obser-
vations conducted during user testing. Each student will be referenced to as a participant
followed by a number: P1, P2,..., P8. The raw data can be found in Appendix C. And
each therapist will be referenced to as a therapist followed by a number: T1, T2,..., T6.
The raw data can be found in Appendix D. A usability test was also conducted with the
therapist (Appendix E) in order to examine the user friendliness of the dashboard.
H1: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness contribute to the per-
ceived usefulness of the system through increased user engagement
and perceived personalisation.
Most students initially lacked a clear understanding of what a digital twin was, with many
unable to define the concept. A few imagined it as a person resembling themselves. After
interacting with the system, most students came to perceive a digital twin as an avatar that
represents them, with some viewing it as an interactive friend. However, one remained
unclear or was unable to express themselves even after using the system. For instance,
P2 encountered technical issues with the avatar. Another student (P6) expressed mixed
feelings, expressing an appreciation of the avatars presence while also enjoying com-
petition against the default opponent. Despite the varied experiences, most participants
recognised the value of regularly using the digital twin system. However, P4 preferred
exercises without VR but indicated they would consider using the avatar if they were to
use the system. Moreover, during the game, the avatar provides voice feedback to inform
and motivate the players. Although most of the students could not understand English,
we observed that they responded positively whenever the avatar included their names.
Hearing their own names made them feel happy and motivated.
The therapists also had varying degrees of initial understanding of what a digital twin
was. Most had some understanding that it was an online copy or a digital avatar, while
one therapist had no idea. After interacting with the system, they generally viewed the
digital twin as an avatar resembling them, with some, like T1 seeing it as an evolving
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
93
==PAGE:103==
interactive object. They generally found the system useful compared to traditional ex-
ercises without VR, particularly for its motivational aspects and improving the students
focus. They also appreciated the systems data visualisation capabilities, such as range
of motion, and the daily collection of data, which provided them with a quantified overview
of the students capabilities, which in contrast is usually reviewed once a year. However,
concerns were also raised about the technical proficiency required, suggesting a need for
detailed introductions for some of their colleagues. And some therapists such as T5 and
T6 mentioned that the systems exercises only covered a portion of the students usual
training, depending on their cognitive level
They highlighted specific features that made the system beneficial, including the automa-
tion of performance assessment, which improved upon their traditional methods of man-
ually counting repetitions. The functionality of daily monitoring the students progress and
record their previous performance was mentioned as being particularly motivating for the
students, allowing them to challenge themselves and track their progress.
Overall through these results, the interaction with the system significantly contributed to
participants understanding of what HDTs are, which is crucial for perceiving the system as
useful. Initially, many participants had vague or no understanding of digital twins. Through
using the system, they generally conceptualised the digital twin as a personalised avatar,
which enhanced their engagement and perception of personalisation within the exercise
process. This improved understanding was essential in recognising the systems ben-
efits and contributed to the perceived usefulness, making the digital twin concept more
relatable. Through these observations, we find support for the hypothesis H1 that HDT el-
ements in serious game-based fitness enhances the perceived usefulness of the system
by increasing user engagement and perceived personalisation, from both the therapists
and the students perspective.
H2: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are associated with the
system’s perceived ease of use.
Most students found it clear that they were playing against themselves in the HDT version
of the bike game, because of the visual and verbal cues. All participants recognised their
digital twin. However, P6 had difficulty interacting with the avatar as it were ahead most
of the time, which reduced the clarity of the HDT. Indicating that while most found the
avatar representation straightforward, some improvements could be made to enhance
clarity. The game ”Jump Jump” was popular for its movement and exercise aspects, as
noted by participants P1, P2, P4 and P7. Others preferred the Syncsense games for their
scenic environment, as highlighted by P1 and P5. They mentioned the interactivity in the
Syncsense games were especially engaging. These preferences generally indicate that
the games were well-received and did not present significant usability issues.
The therapist dashboard was generally viewed as a useful extension of the system, with
its visualisations being intuitive and easy to understand. However, some therapists, T3
and T4 found the dashboard confusing and expressed the need for a better introduction
to the dashboard system. This suggests that while the dashboard is largely user-friendly,
there is a need for improved onboarding and training for new users. Especially the range
of motion data visualisation, two therapists T1 and T4 mentioned that the current version
is too simplistic to accurately represent the real status of mobility. They pointed out that
the arms keeping straight and lifting does not accurately reflect the students upper limb
physical condition because upper limb mobility is more complex than a 2D representation.
I.e some students can raise their upper arms but cannot keep them straight, while others
can raise their forearms but cannot raise their upper arms due to limited movements in
94
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:104==
their shoulders.
The feedback indicates that most students and therapists found the HDT elements and
the overall system intuitive and easy to use, despite some areas needing refinement.
Most users recognised their digital twins and found the game designs clear and enjoyable
as supported by H1. While some therapists experienced confusion with the dashboard
and found the ROM visualisations too simplistic. Therefore, through these observations,
we find mostly support for the hypothesis H2 that HDT elements in serious game-based
fitness does not decrease the systems perceived ease of use, but can be improved upon
to make it even clearer.
H3: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are linked to system
likeability and user enjoyment.
The feedback were generally positive regarding the systems ability to motivate the stu-
dents to complete their exercises. Students particularly enjoyed the bike game for the
competitive nature of the HDT, with P1 and P3 highlighting the sense of challenge and
motivation it provided. Compared with competing with the bike with default speed, the
majority of the students preferred playing against their digital twin in the biking game, as
they found it motivating and enjoyable to compete against their own past performance.
Students P1, P3, P5, P7 and P8 all preferred this aspect of the HDT, as it gave them
a sense of progress and competition. Most of them felt more motivated when using the
games because they also found it fun to explore the virtual world. For instance, P2 and
P6 enjoyed the ability to move and explore the pre-recorded films from Syncsense, which
added an adventures element to their exercises. P8 noted that the game made it easier
for her to use her disabled hand, highlighting it was a unique benefit of the digital twin,
which enhanced her engagement and enjoyment.
However, few students such as P3 and P4 preferred traditional exercises due to their
variety. This indicates that while the HDT elements were engaging for many, individual
preferences for exercise types has a role in the overall enjoyment for the students. More-
over, some therapists indicated that the game JumpJump is not intuitive for students.
Participants often performed the exercise incorrectly in an easier way, bending their arms
or using only their forearms. Therapists often had to remind and correct the incorrect
postures and movements of the students.
The most striking aspects that made the system enjoyable was the ability to compete with
previous performance and move around in virtual worlds which enabled a new adventures
aspects to the exercises. The most common negative feedback were that there was
a lack of variety in the exercises done with the system. Through these observations,
we find strong support for the hypothesis H3 that HDT elements in serious game-based
fitness increases the system likeability and user enjoyment. The HDT elements enhanced
user engagement, with the competitive and exploratory aspects being the most striking
contributors to increased motivation and enjoyment.
H4: HDT elements in serious game-based fitness are related to users’
willingness to incorporate the system into their everyday fitness rou-
tines.
When considering the systems future use, feedback was generally positive. In relation
to usability the first two questions in the usability test addressed the functions of adding
clients and tasks, with all participants selecting either ‘Agree’ or ‘Strongly Agree’, except
one who as ‘Neutral’ about adding tasks. However, two therapists noted that the ‘add
buttons’ were hard to find because they were located at the bottom of the page. The
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
95
==PAGE:105==
ROM data visualisation got mixed feedback. One therapist ‘disagreed’ that the ROM
display was clear and helpful, while three were ’neutral’, and two ‘strongly agreed’. This
mixed feedback highlights areas where the system could be improved to better meet user
needs. However according to Appendix E most were in agreement with the statements,
that the system overall is user friendly enough.
When comparing the incorporation of VR games to the students normal exercises, most of
the students preferred exercising with the games due to the fun and novelty of the digital
environment. For example, P1 found the bike game particularly motivating. The engaging
and novel aspects of the digital twin system were key factors in participants preferences
for game-based training over traditional methods. When asked about implementing the
digital twin system into the students daily exercise routines, most students expressed a
desire to do so, preferring the inclusion of their avatars. This suggests a strong willingness
to adopt the system as part of the students regular fitness sessions.
However, from the therapist perspective they expressed some concerns about the stu-
dents using the system for their daily exercise routines. They acknowledged that while
the system is engaging and beneficial, its primary focus on upper limb exercises might not
be comprehensive enough for students entire bodies. Additionally, the technical complex-
ities of using headsets, sensors and other devices could be a challenge for both students
and therapists. These concerns help explain some of the reservations reflected in the
usability test. Despite technical challenges and the focus solely on upper limbs, the feed-
back from the therapists (Appendix D) indicates an overall willingness to incorporate the
system into their daily routines. For the students the fun, engaging and motivating aspects
of the games along with their personal avatars contributes towards a positive perspective
on the hypothesis. For the therapists, the issues and required improvements are out-
weighed by the overall positive feedback towards using the system in their daily process,
noting that the benefits of HDTs introduce many improvements.
Through these observations, we find a support for the hypothesis that HDT elements in
serious game-based fitness increases the user’s willingness to incorporate the system
into their everyday fitness routines. The positive feedback from students regarding en-
gagement and motivation is in agreement with the therapists usability tests and answers
in the questionnaire about willingness to use the system.
