6
Discussion
This chapter would provide discussion of our research process, findings, and results. The
discussion include reflections and observations from field testing, encompassing user
data collection, accessibility adjustments, function trade-offs, and participant feedback.
These reflections, originating from the testing phase, be discussed in relation to the gen-
eral research question and the sub research question and outlined in Section 1. We
systematically examine and illustrate the research objectives, identifying their outcomes
and relevance to our thesis. Additionally, we outline the limitations and suggest directions
for future research based on our field testing and product implementation.
6.1
Integration of Scrum Methodology
Scrum, applying in our system as the functional project management structure, facilitates
an organised approach to proceed and evaluate our upper-limb fitness system. It serves
as an agile management method, helps us narrow down our MVP based on the the daily
backlog, also offers a cross-functional team collaboration and flexible plans to highlights
the self management for each team member, which is suitable for small team, reducing
the communication costs and improve development efficiency (Section 3.1).
Regarding to the scrum practice, our system follows the principles of Scrum. Though our
system only consists of the UX designer and developers, all members were responsible
for the Scrum practices and maintaining the quality of our product to meet the stakeholder
requirements and maximise the interests. At the early stage of our thesis, we conducted
weekly meetings to discuss user groups and scenarios by reviewing prior theses and re-
lated works. These literature reviews helped us refine our research question and define
the necessary system functions, leading to a preliminary design. Once we had a pre-
liminary design, we began the project development with daily personal discussions and
weekly systematic iterations. Each iteration period (sprint) was planned with clear goals
based on the our meeting records (backlog), which was continuously updated to reflect
the latest findings and requirements. Since the design would changes along with the
refinement of target users and target scenarios, this iterative approach was essential in
ensuring the consistency in development and design, also add some new insights and
challenges in our system.
After completing the development, we moved on to field testing our system. Before each
test, we held meetings to decide on the test content such as what kind of game we would
like to test and how is the test process, and during the tests, we recorded observations
based on the user behaviour and communications between us. We then summarised
these observations twice a week which is same with the testing frequency, and updated
the records accordingly in one shared document. This process allowed us to systemat-
ically identify and address issues, leading to incremental improvements in the system’s
functionality and usability. In this process, we maintain a high level in all aspects of the
software development, then all team members could understand those changes and also
help proceed the inspection and adaptation to ensure the significant deviations from the
process are discovered and figured out in a timely manner.
As we transitioned to the thesis writing phase, we adjusted our schedule to include daily
meetings. This change ensured that we continued iterating on the system to meet user
needs and stakeholder expectations effectively. During the field testing period, there still
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
97
==PAGE:107==
are some functions in system that required to be improved, then these continuous itera-
tions in thesis writing also provided a platform for us to further complete our enhancement
upon performance and functionality in design and development.
The application of Scrum methodology provided a robust foundation for the development
and evaluation of our integrated upper-limb fitness system. The Scrum practices sig-
nificantly enhanced communication within the team, keeping everyone aligned with the
project goals and ensuring a cohesive development process, also built on an environ-
ment for further exploring the potential solutions in healthcare innovation.
6.2
Research Questions
The main research question How can HDT be designed and implemented to enhance
the user experience in serious game-based upper limb fitness is discussed by exam-
ining the sub-research questions.
Regarding to the sub research question How can we design a serious game-based
fitness system using HDT to meet user needs through UX design. We used UCD to
help us involve users throughout the design process, Universal Design theory (Section
2.5) to enable us to make the UI accessible and inclusive, and MVP concept (Section
3.1.1) ensured us to focus on key features first. Then we used Ability Prompt Cards to
ensure our design was inclusive and identified users’ needs in Section 4.1 by persona
and task analysis. Prototypes are used to refine and present our ideas. We also iterated
on the design before finalising the implementation, adjusting based on user feedback and
technical assessment.
Nevertheless, in the task analysis stage (4.1.3), we proposed a potential use scenario
wherein primary users could exercise on their own using the system. The feedback from
therapists indicated that the students are not inclined to exercise independently. We were
unable to verify this use scenario with our testing user group. This shows a gap between
the anticipated needs of our target user group and the actual needs of our testing user
group.
Despite the challenge, the system met several key needs of the users by providing an en-
gaging experience for the primary users in , a helpful tool for therapists and an useful tool
for third-party caregivers. The validation of how the system meets users needs is further
discussed in the last sub-research question. Overall, the results shows the potential of
our system to meet user needs through UX design while also showing areas for further
validation.
For the sub research question How can we design and implement a full-stack archi-
tecture that is both scalable and reliable, ensuring the seamless integration and
operation of serious games, we have discuss it in four different aspects. During the
design phase of our full-stack application, which serves as the feedback system for the
serious game, we focused on creating a simple and user-friendly web-based interface.
This decision was driven by the need for ease of use and reliability, essential for moni-
toring students’ progress and performance clearly. In order to narrow down the functions
in the web application, we did lots of literature reviews to refine them. We consciously
traded off many redundant components and functions to streamline the application. This
trade-off was crucial to enhance usability and maintainability, ensuring that the system
remains intuitive for therapists who use it to follow up and analysis the student progress.
To realise our design, We selected Java Spring Boot, Next.js, and MongoDB as our pri-
mary technologies in web application, considering their capabilities to meet our scalability
98
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:108==
and reliability requirements. The Spring Boot was chosen for its modularity that breaks
down our business into smaller services and ensures the independency between differ-
ent microservices, enabling us to integrate specific functions, such as the MongoDB Java
driver and Redis, improving overall system scalability (Section 2.6.4). And the Next.js
was chosen for the front-end development due to its advanced features and organised
codebase, making it easier to maintain and scale the application as new features are
added. The built-in page routing and authentication mechanisms also simplify the de-
velopment process and ensure the navigation guard and data protection (Section 2.6.2).
Then We opted for MongoDB Atlas, because of its suitability for horizontal scaling. Mon-
goDB’s document-based model allows for flexible data structures without the need for
strict relationships between different collections and its cloud service also allows us to ac-
cess it through link, which is ideal for our cross-platform development with ever-changing
database schema. This flexibility also facilitates data updates and maintenance (Section
2.6.4).
After having implemented our web application, we focused on integrating the web-based
application with the unity game to maintain the data consistency. To let the unity game
retrieve specific data from database as web application did, we adopted the embedded
mobile database - ”MongoDB Realm” in the unity game, which allows the unity to get
access to. But there is an issue regarding to concurrency. Ensuring low-latency com-
munication among different platforms, We achieved this through optimised RESTful APIs
that facilitate efficient data exchange between the game and the web application, then
process the data in the back-end and update in the database.
During field testing, we received positive feedback from therapists who used the web ap-
plication. Most therapists expressed a willingness to use the system regularly, provided
there is comprehensive guidance available. In addition to letting them use our web appli-
cation, we also conducted an interview and survey to collect their feedback and restore
their feelings when using the web application to the greatest extent. The application per-
formed reliable functionality, with no significant performance issues reported. Therapists
appreciated the simplicity and effectiveness of the system in tracking and analysing stu-
dent performance. Even if someone is not used to digital systems, they express their
approval of the usefulness of our application.
By carefully designing and implementing a full-stack architecture using Java Spring Boot,
Next.js, and MongoDB, we achieved a relatively scalable and reliable system for the
seamless integration and operation of serious games, for a user group of this size.
For the sub-research question How can gameplay and a VR-sensor-based platform
be designed and implemented to enhance engagement, accessibility, and effective-
ness in upper-limb fitness?, our game platform features four distinct games—Cycling,
JumpJump, Wheelchair Racing, and Wheelchair Football—each utilising different motion
inputs, including motion intensity, accumulated motion intensity, rotation intensity, and
forward and backward rotation, respectively. Motion data is streamed from lightweight
BLE motion sensors mounted on exercise equipment to the VR headset. The platform
integrates the Human Digital Twin (HDT) concept, incorporating visual avatars that re-
semble the users, a voice companion providing encouragement and feedback, and past
performance mirroring in the Cycling game to allow users to compete against their previ-
ous performances. Additionally, the platform connects to a web-based dashboard via a
MongoDB database, fetching user information at runtime to personalise the experience.
For improving engagement, the game platform has personalised and real-time feedback
with HDT. The platform integrates the HDT concept, providing a highly personalised ex-
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
99
==PAGE:109==
perience. Visual avatars that resemble the users are employed, and a voice companion
offers real-time feedback, calling users by their names and providing encouragement. In
the Cycling game, users can compete against their past performances, motivating con-
tinuous improvement. Besides, the gameplay is also designed in a interesting and im-
merse way. The immersive nature of VR, combined with techniques like Bezier curve for
smooth jumping transitions in JumpJump and Perlin noise for varied track generation in
racing games, ensures that the gameplay remains interesting and fresh. This immersion
distracts users from pain and fatigue, encouraging longer and more effective exercise
sessions. For enhancing accessibility, we integrate our platform seamlessly with existing
equipment. Motion sensors are mounted on standard fitness equipment such as hand cy-
clers, dumbbells, and wheelchairs. Patients can perform their exercises as they normally
would, only with the addition of a VR headset, eliminating the need to learn new tech-
niques. Based on this setting, the gameplay itself is also simple and easy to understand,
utilising straightforward motion movements. The intuitive design allows users to focus on
their fitness exercises without the distraction of complex controls or gameplay mechanics.
By improving engagement and accessibility, the platform encourages users to partici-
pate in fitness exercises with greater effort and willingness, thereby enhancing overall
effectiveness. For example, in the Cycling game, players strive to beat their past per-
formances, fostering a sense of achievement and continuous progress. This increased
motivation and adherence to the exercise regimen lead to better fitness outcomes over
time. In conclusion, the VR-sensor-based game platform for upper-limb fitness, through
its thoughtful implementation and UCD, significantly enhances engagement, accessibility,
and effectiveness. The platform transforms traditional fitness exercises into an engaging
and motivating experience, ultimately improving patient outcomes.
The sub-research question How do different embodiments of the HDT in the devel-
oped system affect its effectiveness and the acceptance of the human digital twin
concept is crucial in understanding how different user groups perceive and comprehend
the HDT concept, and what aspects of embodiment they find most significant. The results
from our study highlight key themes in the usage, acceptance, and perceived usefulness
of a HDT. While not definitive, student feedback consistently pointed to the personalisa-
tion aspect as important. Students frequently mentioned that an HDT was something that
looked like them, indicating that visual resemblance was important for acceptance.
The HDT systems ability to personalised avatars was important in helping the students
form a personal attachment to their digital counterparts. The customisation process was
particularly engaging for the students, as was evident while they were creating their avatars.
While most of the students created avatars that resembled their physical appearance, two
students chose a different visual appearance. One student created an avatar with a dis-
tinct different hair colour she wished to have, and another created one with a different
gender. Despite these differences, both students still identified their avatars as represen-
tations of themselves when they talked about their avatars, and not as separate things
with different identities. Students reactions to seeing their avatars for the first time in the
game were very positive. They immediately recognised the avatars as themselves as
one student mentioned ”Because I can see that it is me”, while also expressing excite-
ment and enjoyment. This emotional bond or personal attachment is a well used method
in video games and was clearly effective in this context. It suggests that the ability to
create and interact with a personalised avatar can significantly enhance engagement and
acceptance of the HDT concept.
Another important aspect is the sense of personal progression through the digital avatar.
100 Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:110==
In traditional video games, progression is often tied to overall level advancement or the
evolution of a single character. In our system, this progression was reflected in the perfor-
mance of the biking opponent and final statistical display in the game. Despite these being
less obvious forms of progression compared to typical gaming methods, one mentioned
”because it better matched my performance” which shows that students still understood
and appreciated the subtle display of evolution in the HDT. They liked competing against
their past performance and felt a sense of improvement, indicating that even minimal in-
dications of progression can be effective. It can also indicate that a more subtle way to
displaying progression can make the digital twin feel more like a reacting being rather
than an superficial digital copy.
The emotional connection students developed with their avatars highlights the potential
of HDT systems to create a meaningful and engaging fitness experience. This bond can
lead to more consistent use and better outcomes, as students are more likely to continu-
ously use the system if they feel a personal connection wit hit. While the current system
effectively supports personal attachment and a sense of progression, there is room for
improvement. Enhancing the clarity and visibility of progression elements could further
increase the students motivation and engagement. Additionally, expanding the range of
customisable features would also likely strengthen the personal attachment, this was clear
when one student said ”It is not me, because I’m blind on my one eye” indicating that he
had a harder time attaching himself to the avatar due to its lack of customisation.
As for the impact of different fitness ways on students, traditional fitness often relies on
therapists for guidance and motivation, making it lack engagement and motivation. But it
offers a wider variety of exercises, which some students preferred for a more comprehen-
sive physical workout, as we collected in Section 5.4. Gamified fitness, where students
competed against a bike with a default speed in the Cycling game. It introduced the
competitive element that increased engagement and motivation compared to traditional
methods. It was received by students, indicating its potential to make fitness more engag-
ing. However, the default speed setting did not provide enough motivation and lacked the
connection to Avatar, which could reduce long-term engagement. Compared with them,
HDT fitness enhanced engagement, as students preferred competing against their past
performance, finding it more motivating and enjoyable.
Therapists initially viewed the HDT as just a digital copy of students. As we mentioned
in Section 5.4, after using the system, they tended to associate the HDT with the sys-
tem behind as a tool with detailed data visualisations, such as ROM and task records,
providing valuable insights for them. They also found that digital twins make students
more engaging and motivated, which helps them work more efficiently, as one mentioned
“I don’t need to keep speaking to them [to keep them motivated] when they’re playing
games”. The competitive elements of the games further improved the motivation. One
indicated that “she [the student] is way faster when playing the Cycling games [than their
daily hand cycling sessions]”. They generally perceived that the system with digital twins
is more convenient than traditional methods. However, some therapists found the ROM
visualisations too simple because the mobility of the upper limbs in real situations is com-
plicated. For example, some student can raise their upper arms but can not keep them
straight, and some can raise their forearms but can not raise their upper arms due to
shoulder movement limitations. It is suggested to provide a more detailed way to present
the upper-limb physical conditions. Technical skills were also a concern, suggesting the
need for training and reducing the complexity of the set up devices. Additionally, when
the game involved more flexible movement, such as using dumbbells, students often ex-
hibited incorrect movements, requiring more guidance from therapists. This highlights the
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
101
==PAGE:111==
need for clearer instructions within the UI of the game.
Overall, therapists appreciated HDT for its record for exercise process and assessments
of physical condition, which improved rehabilitation. By improving in data visualisation
and giving more comprehensive guidance in games, therapists can gain even more value
from HDT.
As for third-party caregivers, here we discuss the prior research that was done on them
since we did not get any data directly from them, even though we sent emails with system
invitations and online surveys to them after user testing.
According to Lauer-Schmaltz et al. and Praetorius et al., visualisation or even human-like
objects based on the embodiment of HDTs could enhance understanding of their under-
lying data and support emotional relations with their primary users, patients [12, 115].
Additionally, Lauer-Schmaltz et al. indicated that demonstrating the patient’s ROM and
replaying patient movements from recorded exercise sessions was considered an intu-
itive way to provide a sense of the patient’s physical condition [12]. Although our project
does not include replaying patient movements from recorded exercise sessions, we pro-
vide ROM and exercise processes and complete status to help them know the informa-
tion. The preview videos of the games also could help them understand the gameplay,
which aids in understanding the primary users’ exercise contents. Moreover, according
to the paper, The avatar’s visual association with the patient could improve respondents’
engagement with dashboard applications, giving them the impression of working with the
patient rather than just collecting data [12]. During our user testing, most students created
Avatars that looked like them, and this could give a visual association to third-party care-
givers. Moreover, the giving thumbs-up functionality in our system increases interactivity
and has the potential to increase their sense of engagement.
Overall, previous research suggests that similar systems to ours could provide third-party
caregivers with a better understanding of primary users’ conditions and give better en-
gagement, but this requires further validation with real user testing for ours.
6.3
Limitations and Future works
Despite the achievements in designing and implementing a scalable and reliable HDT-
based serious game product, several limitations were identified during testing phases.
These limitations need to be considered to understand the constraints and areas for im-
provement in our product.
One of the primary limitations was the small sample size and low testing frequency. The
field testing was conducted over a period of only five days within three weeks with nine
students and six therapists. This short duration and limited sample size are insufficient to
ensure the data accuracy and reliability necessary for large-scale deployment. Especially
for the therapist participants, they only test the web application once due the technical
issue at the early stage of testing phase and the time limitation. Though it does prove
the usefulness of our product to a certain extent, these sample data are too small and
limited to be generalisable among larger user group. Then, to avoid such circumstance
that we have discussed above, we might need to extend our testing period and increase
frequency in the future, making our observations and reflections more perceivable and
more suitable for amounts of different groups who might use our product.
Additionally, our testing group focusing on a specific area in the Maglemosen, includes
not only those young students with upper-limb impairments but also with cognitive impair-
ments, which is different from what we assumed in our target user group initially (Section
102 Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:112==
4.1.1). Moreover, most students in the field testing required physical support from their
therapists while playing the games and all our communication needs to be relayed by the
therapist because of the language barrier, presenting significant challenges in maintaining
high user compliance and ensuring the accuracy of our data collection, especially during
extended fitness sessions. The first gap between our testing group and initial target pri-
mary users is the age range. Young students are unlike adults and elderly individuals
with upper-limb impairment, who have higher independence in life. As therapists have
mentioned, students often lack the motivation to exercise on their own. The second gap
is that the physical and cognitive conditions of the testing group might not fully represent
the broader target group, as their needs may not be as diverse as the wider population
with upper limb impairments. For future studies, it is recommended to cover a wide range
of user groups with varying levels of abilities and conditions during user testing.
Regarding to the data collection, the criteria were not well-defined in the early stages,
leading to inconsistencies and inaccuracies in the data collection. Without clear intentions
and methodologies, the data collected in the beginning of the field testing was unsuitable
for further thorough analysis and assessment. Moreover, uncontrollable environmental
setup during testing resulted in occasional sensor mismatches, further affecting the ac-
curacy and reliability of the exercise data.
For the evaluation, we did not include clinical testing about the effectiveness of the fitness
system, as we are not professional clinical researchers. While our user testing provided
valuable insights into engagement and usability, it did not provide the clinical evidence to
assess the system’s clinical benefits. For future research, considering collaborating with
clinical professionals to conduct studies could provide more comprehensive insights into
the system.
There are several limitations with the HDT embodiment in game platform described in
Section 4.3.4. Our current embodiment techniques encompass three primary methods:
visual avatar representation, voice companion interaction, and performance mirroring. In
terms of visual avatar representation (Section 4.3.4.1), our games currently place the
user’s avatar in the front during gameplay. To enhance user engagement, it would be
beneficial to integrate the avatars with more dynamic movement animations and can also
include personalised configuration of animations for better movement mirroring . It could
serve as a personalised exercising guide for users, reducing the need for external su-
pervision and improving engagement. Regarding the voice companion (Section 4.3.4.2),
the system currently activates voice interactions only in response to specific, hard-coded
events within the game. Additionally, the speaking content is predefined for all users, with
only the user’s name being dynamically inserted. To enhance engagement and make the
HDT more vivid, AI can be used to generated various sentences at runtime with a voice
that sounds like users. Currently, the interaction is unidirectional, flowing from the HDT
to the user. Future improvements could enable bidirectional communication, allowing
users to chat with the HDT, give instructions, and express preferences to create a bet-
ter environment for uses to interact with AI more frequently and intelligently throughout
the gameplay experience. AI can also study past conversation and other user informa-
tion from the database and act as a more personalised coach. Performance mirroring
is currently implemented solely in our Cycling game (Section 4.3.4.3), as this game is
the most popular among our test users and intuitively facilitates racing against another
bike. However, performance embodiment presents significant design challenges in other
games, such as JumpJump, which lack a racing mechanism. This limitation underscores
a fundamental issue with performance embodiment, as it primarily applies to games fea-
turing competitive elements, such as racing or achieving high scores. Consequently, this
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
103
==PAGE:113==
restricts the scenarios in which performance mirroring can be utilised.
Our game is based on a VR platform, which provides players with an immersive and en-
gaging experience. However, the use of VR technology also presents several drawbacks.
Wearing a VR headset can be uncomfortable for some users, especially if it is used for
more than 30 minutes, as it tends to be heavy. During our testing, we observed that some
participants struggled to keep their heads steady, leading to misalignment in the game
and a shaky view within the VR headset. The high sensitivity of VR systems, designed
to accurately detect and simulate user movements, often brings physical discomfort and
cybersickness mentioned in Section 2.6.6.1. Additionally, while VR headsets are feasible
for exercises that do not require full body movement, such as hand cycling, they are im-
practical for activities involving jumping or rolling. Consequently, although integrating VR
into fitness exercises can enhance effectiveness for certain types of activities, it is not suit-
able for all exercises and may introduce discomfort and cybersickness. Alternatives such
as voice guidance or monitor-based games could allow a wider range of exercises and
minimise the side effects associated with the use of external devices, offering potential
solutions to the limitations of VR.
Although our game currently uses motion sensors for input, the game platform still requires
a hand controller to configure settings and start the game. This method allows for precise
control and easy development. However, using a hand controller can be challenging for
some users and inconvenient, as they need to fetch the controller for UI control and then
put it back to play the game. There are several alternative control methods that could be
explored to enhance user convenience, such as gesture control, gaze control, and voice
control. These approaches could provide a more intuitive and accessible way to manage
the game platform. Therefore, more design consideration should be given to developing
a straightforward and user-friendly control method for the game platform.
We also face limitations regarding motion mirroring and adaptability. While the motion
sensor is accurate and sensitive, the game input relies on a one-to-one mapping. For
instance, after reaching an upper boundary, the player cannot accelerate further in the
game, even if they move the sensor faster. This implementation aims to stabilise and
control the game better, but it can diminish the motivation for intense exercise, as players
can achieve the same speed with less effort.
Additionally, our game system does not account for individual differences in users’ motion
capabilities. While we have a feature where the cycling opponent can mirror a player’s
past performance, the conversion from motion sensor data to game input is uniform across
all users. It would be more effective and personalised to develop a system that calibrates
and adapts to different user abilities. This way, a stronger user would need to move faster
to achieve the same speed compared to an average user, thereby promoting a more
tailored exercise experience.
104 Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
==PAGE:114==
7
Conclusion
The platform implemented to examine the main research question How can Human Dig-
ital Twins be designed and implemented to enhance the user experience in serious
game-based upper limb fitness? was designed using design principles and iterative
prototyping to develop an accessible and inclusive HDT-based fitness system. This al-
lowed us to tailor the design to meet the needs of the primary users, therapists and third-
party caregivers. We implemented a full-stack system with technical frameworks focusing
on reliability and scalability. Connected to the web-based dashboard was the VR platform,
our system integrated the HDT concept to provide a highly personalised experience. It
featured user avatars created in the web dashboard, a voice companion offering real-time
personalised feedback, and performance mirroring in the cycling game to allow users to
compete against their previous performance. The system demonstrated real-world func-
tionality with actual participants trying the entire system. Despite not being tested on a
larger scale, the system provided participants with a better understanding of what an HDT
is, exposing them to its potential. This showcases our systems ability to work in a real
environment and indicating its potential for a broader application and further development.
The findings from the testing resulted in different feedback. Among the students, the
personalised aspect of their own created 3D avatars was the most essential outcome
from the testing. This embodiment of the HDT concept allowed students to choose and
personalise their own avatars to reflect their identities. The personal attachment to the
avatars helped them see the progress of their HDT as their own, making their progression
in the game more personal and meaningful. By seeing their avatars perform similarly to
themselves, they were more likely to feel connected to their virtual representation, en-
hancing their engagement and motivation to participate actively in the exercise sessions.
In contrast, therapists appreciated the HDT’s exercise records, physical assessments,
and convenience of management. The HDT also provided an immersive environment
that attracted the students attention, reducing the therapists workload and allowing them
more time for planning fitness programs. For third-party users, the HDT elements were
similar to those for the therapists, exercise records and physical assessments, but with
visualisations simplified for non-professional users. This increased their feeling of being
part of the process rather than observing as external participants.
This thesis explored how a HDT can be designed and implemented to enhance the user
experience in serious game-based upper limb fitness. The feedback indicated promising
results in terms of enhancing the user experience in upper limb fitness, with game ele-
ments that successfully engages the users, a platform that supports these elements, and
a design that is user friendly. Additionally, the therapists also indicated a strong interest
and positive first initial impression of the system and its usefulness. However, the results
also revealed limitations, and further area for improvement. Considering the limitations
and future work, such as the exercises were limited to upper limb, users not being able
to operate the headset independently, data visualisations not being detailed enough, etc.
Therefore, this thesis offers a great foundation for future HDT system for fitness.

Engaging Workout

C
Appendix: Student Interviews
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
117
==PAGE:127==
The summarise of questions
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin?
Most participants had no initial idea of what a digital twin is (P1, P3, P4, P5, P6, P8).
Some imagined it as someone who looks like them (P2, P7).
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin now that you tried our system?
Participants generally understood a digital twin as something that looks like them after using the
system (P1, P2, P3, P4, P5).
One participant saw it as an avatar she could identify with, although still not entirely clear (P6).
One participant could not answer (P7)
Another participant described it as someone you can do things with (P8).
Which of the games did you enjoy the most (and why)?
Enjoyment was spread across different games: Jump Jump (P1, P2, P4, P7), Bike (P1, P3),
Tivoli (P1, P5), Animal Park (P1, P6), and amusement park settings (P1, P8).
One liked all (P1)
because he liked the movement/exercise aspect of it. (P2)
The bike game, because of the competition aspect. (P3)
Jump jump, because of the movement, but also because of the in-game world. (P4)
Tivoli game because of scenery (P5)
Liked all syncsense games the most (P6)
Jump jump. It was funniest to cycle. But the reason was that is was funnier to jump around (P7)
She likes all of them, but probably she likes the amusement park most, because of the settings
with the animals and the amusement park. (P8)
Did you enjoy training with the games more than your normal exercises?
==PAGE:128==
Majority preferred training with games due to fun and novelty (P1, P2, P5, P6, P7, P8).
The bike game was very motivating. (P1)
Some preferred traditional exercises for variety and motivation (P3, P4).
Were you more motivated than in your normal exercises? If so, what made it more
motivating?
Majority felt more motivated with the games due to fun and exploration (P1, P2, P3, P5, P6, P7).
One participant preferred traditional exercises for their variety (P4).
One participant found it easier to use her disabled hand in the game (P8).
In the biking game, did you prefer playing against the default bike or playing against your
digital twin?
Majority preferred playing against the digital twin (P1, P3, P5, P7, P8).
One participant had technical issues with the avatar (P2).
Mixed feelings about the avatar versus the other bike (P6).
Why did you prefer playing against the digital twin? What makes it so special?
Participants found it motivating and fun to play against themselves (P1, P3, P4, P5, P6, P7, P8).
Specific reasons included knowing they were competing against their past performance and the
avatar's appearance.
Was it clear to you that in the HDT-version of the bike game you played against yourself?
If so, what made it clear?
Majority found it clear due to visual and verbal cues (P1, P2, P3, P4, P5, P7, P8).
One participant's avatar was ahead most of the time, affecting interaction (P6).
==PAGE:129==
Did you find the avatar confusing?
Most did not find the avatar confusing and identified with it (P1, P2, P3, P4, P5, P7, P8).
One participant had mixed feelings but appreciated the avatar's presence (P6).
Would you like to have systems like those in your daily rehabilitation process? If so,
would you prefer to have the system with the digital twin or without?
Majority would like to incorporate the system into their daily rehabilitation, preferring the
inclusion of their avatar (P1, P2, P3, P5, P6, P7, P8).
One participant preferred traditional rehabilitation but would include the avatar if using the
system (P4).

towards that answer)

HDT-version of the system.

(personal) Digital Twin?
No idea

Something that looks like her.

She liked all games.

Yes, it was fun to be in a digital world.

motivating?
The bike game was very motivating.
PQ5: In the biking game, did you prefer playing against the default bike or playing
against your digital twin?
She preferred playing against her Digital Twin, particularly because she knew she was playing
against herself.

the digital twin? What makes it so special? (see above)

yourself? If so, what made it clear?

I was more motivated. It was because it was inside an environment.
PQ5: In the biking game, did you prefer playing against the default bike or playing
against your digital twin?
It was funnier to play against my digital twin.
PQ6: (If they prefer playing against the Digital Twin) Why did you prefer playing against
the digital twin? What makes it so special?
It was nice to play against myself. And its also weird when theres no one on the bike
Questions addressing H2:
PQ7: Was it clear to you that in the HDT-version of the bike game you played against
yourself? If so, what made it clear?
It was clear.
PQ8: Did you find the avatar confusing?
It was clear because both I were told but also because I could see my avatar
Questions addressing H3:
PQ2, PQ3, PQ5
Questions addressing H4:
PQ9: Would you like it to have systems like those in your daily rehabilitation process? If
so, would you prefer to have the system with the digital twin or without?
Would play with the game and have the digital twin.
Participant 8 (P8)

The summarise of questions
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin?
Most participants had some initial idea of what a digital twin is, being an online copy (T1, T2,
T4, T5, T6)
Some had no idea what it was (T3)
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin now that you’ve worked with our
system?
Participants generally understood a digital twin as something that looks like them after using
the system (T2, T3, T4, T5, T6)
Some went further and understood it as something you could evolve with (T1)
T1: But she couldn’t have imagined the option that you could play against your digital twin.
T4, T6: Some virtual person that you can create. It doesn’t necessarily look like you.
Compared to traditional rehabilitation, how would you rate our system’s usefulness
for rehabilitation?
Found the system useful (T1, T2, T3, T4, T5, T6)
T1: concerned that many colleagues might not be very technically experienced, so it should
have a very detailed introduction.
T2: 'Now, I don’t need to remind them all the time.'
T4: depending on their cognitive capabilities.
T5, T6: only covers a little bit of what they usually train.
What were the elements that made it particularly useful?
Data visualisation and collection (T1, T4)
Making exercise more enjoyable (T2, T3, T5, T6)
T1, T4: The data visualizations would help have a quantified overview of patients’
capabilities on a daily basis (usually they only have yearly assessments).
T3: It is more motivating for the students.
T5: The competition fosters motivation and the biking game was easy to understand and
intuitive.
T6: Particularly the games because they can challenge themselves and it motivates them.
Does the system have any particular benefits for you as a therapist?
==PAGE:147==
Monitoring progress on a daily basis (T1, T2, T3, T4, T5, T6)
T2: Monitoring progress and performance on a daily basis instead of yearly assessments
could be beneficial.
T4: In traditional rehabilitation, they observe and assess performance by counting
repetitions, which is easier with our system as it automates it.
T5: The dashboards are very suitable for documentation and performance/progress
monitoring purposes.
Is the visualization of the students’ performance data within the therapist dashboard a
useful extension to the system? If so why?
Simple to understand (T1, T2, T3, T4, T5, T6)
T1: Yes, the visualizations were obvious and intuitive.
Did you find the therapist dashboard intuitive and easy to use?
Intuitive to understand(T1, T2, T5, T6)
Difficult to understand (T3, T4)
T3: They are a little bit confusing. She would need some minutes to learn how to read the
graphs.
T4: Not really, but she’s also not great with technical systems. Would need a better
introduction to the system.
Overall did you like the system? What could have been improved?
More statistical data (T1, T3, T4)
More difficulty adjustment (T2)
More in game guidance (T5, T6)
T4: Have the possibility to not only state the physiological facts but also add context such as
mood and cognitive condition, as these can significantly influence daily performance. And
ROM visualization is not make a lot sense now, because one degree’s mobility with straight
arms cannot really show the mobility of arms/shoulders of students.
T6: The in-game instructions of Jump Jump in the VR glasses were difficult to understand.
Cycle is good
Would you want to use similar systems on a daily basis in your job as a therapist?
Yes would like to (T1, T5, T6)
==PAGE:148==
Yes but with further improvements (T2, T3, T4)
T2: If the technology works fine, particularly because the system motivates and somewhat
automates the rehabilitation process for the students.
T3: Yes, it would be nice because it’s something new and an easy way to provide students
with an engaging experience without having to come up with creative ideas themselves.
T4: It could be nice to use the system sometimes as an addition to traditional rehabilitation,
but a dashboard like the one we have would be nice.
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin?
T1: A copy of yourself in a digital world.
T2: An online me.
T3: No idea what it is.
T4: Something like the Sims Game.
T5: An avatar that looks like her.
T6: Some kind of figure in the computer that looked like her.
What do you imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin now that you’ve worked with our
system?
T1: Probably kind of the same. But she couldn’t have imagined the option that you could play
against your digital twin.
T2: Doesn’t change her impression.
T3: Your own character in the game. A virtual entity that looks like you and with which you
can interact.
T4: Some virtual person that you can create. It doesn’t necessarily look like you.
T5: An avatar that looks like her.
T6: An avatar that looks like her. An avatar that people can design to look as much like them
as possible.
Compared to traditional rehabilitation, how would you rate our system’s usefulness
for rehabilitation?
T1: It is useful especially for the motivation of the students and concentration. She would use
the system but is concerned that many colleagues might not be very technically
experienced, so it should have a very detailed introduction.
T2: It is useful for the students because it challenges them and offers more options for
rehabilitation than traditional methods. She feels students perform better than usual. For the
cycle game, she said 'Now, I don’t need to remind them all the time.'
T3: It is nice as it is more modern and new, giving context to the exercises for the students.
==PAGE:149==
T4: Yes, overall the system would be useful for some students, depending on their cognitive
capabilities.
T5: It is very useful but at the moment it only covers a little bit of what they usually train.
T6: Yes, it would be useful for some of the students, depending on their cognitive condition.
For therapists, it would also be useful for some of the students.
What were the elements that made it particularly useful?
T1: The data visualizations would help have a quantified overview of patients’ capabilities on
a daily basis (usually they only have yearly assessments).
T2: The digital environments in the games because they give the exercises context.
T3: It is more motivating for the students.
T4: For therapists, the big benefit is having the results to compare with when the students
started (progress). For students, it is more motivating and gives the exercise context.
T5: The competition fosters motivation and the biking game was easy to understand and
intuitive.
T6: Particularly the games because they can challenge themselves and it motivates them.
Does the system have any particular benefits for you as a therapist?
T1: The data visualizations provide a quantified overview of patients’ capabilities on a daily
basis.
T2: Monitoring progress and performance on a daily basis instead of yearly assessments
could be beneficial.
T3: It is very nice to get an overview of the patient’s condition and progress.
T4: In traditional rehabilitation, they observe and assess performance by counting
repetitions, which is easier with our system as it automates it.
T5: The dashboards are very suitable for documentation and performance/progress
monitoring purposes.
T6: For the students that would use these kinds of exercises, it is useful to monitor and
compare their performances and progress.
Is the visualization of the students’ performance data within the therapist dashboard a
useful extension to the system? If so why?
T1: Yes, the visualizations were intuitive and easy to understand.
T2: Yes, the visualizations were obvious and intuitive.
T3: Both the possibility to manage students and track progress makes it useful.
T4: The visualizations themselves are intuitive and easily understandable.
T5: The visualizations in the dashboards were intuitive.
T6: Yes, the visualizations were very intuitive.
==PAGE:150==
Did you find the therapist dashboard intuitive and easy to use?
T1: It was intuitive. The visualizations etc. were not confusing and easy to understand.
T2: The dashboard was easy to use and the visualizations were obvious and intuitive.
T3: They are a little bit confusing. She would need some minutes to learn how to read the
graphs.
T4: Not really, but she’s also not great with technical systems. Would need a better
introduction to the system.
T5: It was very intuitive and easy to use.
T6: It was relatively easy to use. The visualizations were very intuitive.
Overall did you like the system? What could have been improved?
T1: She liked it and knows that many students liked it, particularly the competitive
component in the bike game. In the dashboard, it would have been nice to see a weekly
performance summary for every game to see the progress.
T2: The games could have more diverse challenges (e.g., go uphill and downhill in the bike
game). And for the dashboard, she wants to choose the difficulty level when adding games
tasks.
T3: She would like to see more stats about the individual sessions (e.g., performance
duration).
T4: Have the possibility to not only state the physiological facts but also add context such as
mood and cognitive condition, as these can significantly influence daily performance. And
ROM visualization is not make a lot sense now, because one degree’s mobility with straight
arms cannot really show the mobility of arms/shoulders of students.
T5: Maybe adding something about a goal system. This would be more interesting for the
students so that they can see that they achieved something.
T6: The in-game instructions of Jump Jump in the VR glasses were difficult to understand.
Cycle is good
Would you want to use similar systems on a daily basis in your job as a therapist?
T1: Yes, absolutely.
T2: If the technology works fine, particularly because the system motivates and somewhat
automates the rehabilitation process for the students.
T3: Yes, it would be nice because it’s something new and an easy way to provide students
with an engaging experience without having to come up with creative ideas themselves.
T4: It could be nice to use the system sometimes as an addition to traditional rehabilitation,
but a dashboard like the one we have would be nice.
T5: Yes, definitely.
==PAGE:151==
T6: Yes, for some of the students.

Therapist Interviews (23.05.2024)
Therapist 1
Procedure: Therapists supervised the students during their sessions with the system. During
the third session, they interacted with the therapist dashboard, where they were able to
monitor the student’s condition through data visualizations.
Entry Questions:
TQ0 (Asked at the beginning of the whole experiment session): What do you imagine is a
(personal) Digital Twin?
A copy of yourself in a digital world.
TQ1: What do imagine is a (personal) Digital Twin now that you’ve worked with our system?
Probably kind of the same. But she couldn’t have imagined the option that you could play
against your digital twin.
Questions addressing H1:
TQ2: Compared to traditional rehabilitation, how would you rate our system’s usefulness for
rehabilitation? It is useful especially for the motivation of the students and concentration. She
would use the system, but she is concerned about that many colleagues might not be very
technically experiences, so it should have a very detailed introduction.
TQ3: What were the elements that made it particularly useful? The data visualizations would
help them have a quantified overview of patients’ capabilities on a daily basis (usually they
only have yearly assessments)
TQ4: Does the system have any particular benefits for you as a therapist? See above
==PAGE:152==
TQ5: Is the visualization of the students’ performance data within the therapist dashboard a
useful extension to the system? If so, why?
Questions addressing H2:
TQ6: Did you find the therapist dashboard intuitive and easy to use? It was intuitive. The
visualizations etc. were not confusing and easy to understand.
TQ7: Did the therapist dashboard add any clarity to the rehabilitation process? See above.
Questions addressing H3:
TQ8: Overall, did you like the system? What could have been improved?
She liked it and she knows that many of the students liked it, particularly she feels like the
competitive component in the bike game was very motivating for students. In the dashboard,
it would have been nice to see a weekly performance summary for every game, so that you
can see the progress.
Questions addressing H4:
TQ9: Would you want to use similar systems on a daily basis in your job as therapist? Yes,
absolutely.
Therapist 2
Procedure: Therapists supervised the students during their sessions with the system. During
the third session, they interacted with the therapist dashboard, where they were able to
monitor the student’s condition through data visualizations.
Entry Questions:

feels like the students perform better than usual.

digital environments because it gives the exercises a context.

and mainly exercise at home. You need to add him to the system and assign tasks for him.
Please follow the guide below to complete two tasks:
Task 1: Add the New Student to the System
Basic Information
Name: Jack Jensen
Birth Date: 12-03-2009
Phone Number: 43000000
Email: Jack12@gmail.com
Biological Sex: Male
Recovery Plan
Type of Movement Impairment: (You can leave this empty)
Dominant Arm: Left
Therapy Goals: (You can leave this empty)
Contact Person
Name: Allison
Email: allison123@gmail.com
Phone Number: 45000000
Task 2: Add Weekly Tasks for Jack
You have completed the first therapy session with Jack, and he has learned how to use the system
and devices. Now, you need to add tasks for him. He will exercise by himself at home before the next
therapy session.
Please input the following details into the dashboard and save:

Total Sets: 4
Task Period: 23-5-2024 to 31-5-2024
Cycle
Total Sets: 2
Task Period: 23-5-2024 to 31-5-2024

G
Appendix: Emobidment model by Zhao
and Vande Moere
Figure G.1: The effectiveness of a data sculpture is partly determined by the metaphorical
distance between the data and its physical representation. A shorter distance means the
representation is more intuitive and easier for users to relate to and understand [19].
Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout
157
==PAGE:167==
H
Appendix: The Emoji Questions
For the emoji questions about games (Figure H.1), we designed for students to rate game
preferences After playing each of the games. On the first day, none of the students chose
neutral or negative options. we observed that students tended to choose positive feed-
back, likely to avoid hurting our feelings since they made their selections in our presence.
As a result, we realised this method might not provide valuable feedback. Therefore, we
removed this part after the first day and focused more on the feedback collection of the
last day.
Figure H.1: Sample feedback question for Cycling game using emoji options (The same
options were used for other games)
The feedback question for Avatars was presented to students at the last user testing day
with the interviews. The purpose of this question was to explore whether they had negative
impressions of the avatar, such as confusion and fear. The answer form students include,
5 chose glad, 1 chose glad and motivated, 2 chose neutral. The results showed that they
were positive and neutral, so we did not discuss this further.
Figure H.2: Feedback question for Avatars
158 Universal Design of Future Fitness Systems: Leveraging Human Digital Twins and Serious Games for
Engaging Workout

Field testing log for Maglemosen
Day 1: Monday 6 May
Setup day for each of the participants and introduction to the system 
for the therapists
Logbook
Joey Ho
The therapist seemed to be very interested in the idea and are motivated to give it a real try
In explaining the dashboard to the therapist, I found that a lot of the user input fields were 
redundant and not required. 
I also found that the dashboard were a replication of their current system and would require 
them to do double work in order to try out our dashboard system
The dashboard needs to integrate some kind of requirement for the therapist to want to use the 
system, otherwise they would set a task that lasts the whole testing period and not using the 
system
But if they were to use it, we would need some kind of system for them to write comments and 
logbook on each of their workout sessions
 
When starting a game, it tells you to move and this tricks the players into thinking that they are 
beginning to move, but its only to start the count down.

Day 2: Tuesday 7 May
Testing day with the whole system
Logbook
Joey Ho
Most users seemed interested in having themselves copied into the game and representing 
themselves. One even chose to be a female even though he were a male, due to it being the 
female version of himself, this way he was able to be represented differently than he actually is. 
This will be interesting to observe next time when they see their avatars in game, how they 
would react towards it.
The therapists were very active in keeping them engaged with conversations and questions. A 
great replacement for our game not having that much in game communication.
We need to introduce the therapist dashboard for them, so they can schedule their users 
activities.
We need to plan out how this should work for the therapists.
Overall the day went great, given the issues we still have. We were able to create avatars for all 
those who came, this process was kind of mixed. It was much about how each persons 
personalities were, some were very open, others shy. 
Its difficult for the users to see how far progress they’ve made, therefore either we can add 
curves to the game path so its easier to distinguish when you are moving or not. Or simply zoom 
out of the mini map, so you can see from start to end. That way its possible to track your 
progress.
The therapist said it was normal to always move the students out of their wheelchairs, the more 
the better.
==PAGE:176==
Haoming Yang
Practical: prepare for headset, sensors, equipment (battery, screencast, sound volume), ask 
feedback,
Games: make sure four games work
Yang Chen
Considering the regular exercise most students would do for rehabilitation, our games seem to 
not need the therapist to guide them a lot.
They like the game with competition more.
The game view would follow the movements given by the students’ head, but some students 
could not control the position of the head, just to reduce the sensibility.
We need to address the importance of the avatar in the game to make the avatar outstanding.
Ziwei Chen
The voice of Avatar didn’t work as expected, for example when going half way, users should get 
a reminder, otherwise they feel confused about their progress. The bike game and wheelchair 
game do not have halfway and 90% reminders.
Today, no user surpasses the default avatar performance. So we might need to slow that or 
adjust it to their performance today.
Most of the students created the avatar that looked like themselves.
Day 3: Tuesday 14 May
Testing day with the whole system
Logbook
Joey Ho
P7 has difficulties speaking verbally, but I noticed when she was playing the Syncsense game 
that she was very neutral in her facial expression. But looked much happier in her expression.
The participants seem very interested in VR.
==PAGE:177==
Tille was very happy about being able to see her own avatar and that it was her; but noted that 
the avatar said her name wrong, because we did a mistake and switched between her and P7's 
avatar.
P9: doesn't feel very connected to the avatar since he did not actively himself make it, but was 
the therapist who did most of it. (he was also the one who commented he couldn't choose an 
avatar with an eye patch, because he's blind on one eye)
A lot of issues with connection to the sensors and having the games works correctly. This is 
difficult to mitigate as the users themselves cannot use the controller. So when something 
doesn’t work, its very abrupt, we need to take off the glasses and then fix it and put the glasses 
back on. (Issues with the sensors as it was rotation differently when we tried to do the cycling 
with the legs, this we need to test next time before we try with the legs.)
“Says it doesn’t make sense” with having the avatar cheering on him. (Therapist notices that he 
actually says that about a lot of stuff, so its kind of his way to joke and make fun)
We had to stop early, because his time was up but he mentioned that he wasn't at the goal yet.
P8: no comments on any of the concepts of “digital twin”. Avatar issues: wasn't able to fetch it 
she mentioned that it was “sad” that we couldn't fetch it. She's happy that we can actually 
display the avatar in the end. Does get a little frustrated that the avatar is pushing her, even 
though she's doing her hardest and is maybe tired.
Says she doesn't feel bad or gets bad.
P6: a little bit sad that she was not able to see her own avatar since its the first time and the DB 
was not working. But she still seemed pretty happy with trying it.
P2: was ready to play as soon as possible, he was happy that he could see his avatar created 
again. He remembered that he created his alternative female version of him.

Day 4: Thursday 16 May
Testing day with the whole system
Logbook
Martin:
-
When P9 noticed that there was the opponent bike but that it was not capable of 
catching up with him he actively messed with it (i.e., he always waited until it caught up 
and then drove away again, observing it disappear behind him) -> He didn’t perceive the 
default opponent as an actual challenge and mentioned that he would prefer the bike to 
be ahead of him to challenge him to go faster. But he also highlighted that it was fun 
nevertheless that there is another bike in the game and that this alone was already 
motivating him to actively and voluntarily engage with the rehabilitation (the therapist 
mentioned that he normally is not very motivated and that they to keep telling him to 
keep going during the exercises)
-
Also P8  enjoyed racing the bike, but also noted that it is too easy. But she liked the 
syncsense game more because she’s not a competitive person
-
Also P3 highlighted that racing the opponent bike is more motivating than without an 
opponent, but that the bike itself is not very challenging (in the default mode)
-
For P6, the therapist explained that she typically loses focus quickly, starts talking and 
forgets to keep going with the exercise, so the therapists always have to remind her to 
keep going with the exercise. Therefore, the therapist said that she was super super 
surprised that P6 was very invested and focused in the game for the whole 10 minutes, 
always wanting to catch up with the opponent's bike. However, also P6 explained that 
the other bike is too slow for her (and that is even though the therapist highlighted that 
P6 typically is not a competitive person). Overall, P6 liked our bike game more than the 
syncsense game as it allows her to get as fast as she can
==PAGE:180==
-
P7 doesn’t perceive the bike opponent as challenging but enjoys it as she likes to be 
ahead. She’s also not a competitive person. Also P7 enjoyed our bike game more than 
the syncsense game because of the in-game companion/opponent
Yang Chen
RD:
1.
How far do testers use our system?  Utility→ Attitude→Intention→Actual use
a.
Are the system and game easy to use?
b.
Have they figured out what is HDT?
c.
Do this system and game meet their requirements?
d.
Are they prone to use the system and play those games in the future?
e.
How does HDT help to improve the rehabilitation experience in the game?
Ziwei Chen
-
We emphisized the opponent bike to students before game, and letting them look 
back/left to see the another bike, getting the sense of competition, their behaviors and 
feedbacks show that race makes them more motivated than their daily exercise, but 
most of them feel this default bike is not challenging, they sometimes stop to wait for the 
bike exceed themselves and exceed it again. 
-
We really need to have the customised opponent with their preview speed ready for next 
week,  so we can tell them, you’re competing with previews you, and getting the 
feedback really related to HTD and our topic, which is primary. 
-
Next week, we’ll have game sessions and collect feedback for therapists dashboard on 
Tuesday, and have the final one round of the hand cycle game(competing with their 
preview speed) and 30 mins final interview on Thursday.

•  The  VR  glasses  can  cause  slight  dizziness  –  this  symptom  is

Guide
That  is  when  you  wear  the  VR  glasses,  you  look  ahead.
different  virtual  interaction  e.g
